When a Game Animal Cannot Be Slaughtered: Hanafi Ruling and Shafi'i Dispute
Ruling and dispute on game with significant life when slaughter is impossible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a game animal with more life when slaughter is impossible and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Once game comes into hand, it is no longer game; emergency slaughter no longer applies.

This lesson addresses a scenario where a hunter catches a game animal alive, and it retains more life than a slaughtered animal, but he cannot perform proper slaughter. The Hanafi school rules it not halal, while al-Shafi'i permits it under time constraint. We will trace the reasoning and identify the practical difference.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: the animal is not halal without proper slaughter, regardless of whether the inability is due to lack of tool or time constraint.
This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view.
2
Identify the opposing school and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds it halal if the inability is due to time constraint, because the cause for emergency slaughter exists.
Some later Hanafis also adopt this view as istihsan.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Al-Shafi'i argues that time constraint prevents proper slaughter, so the darurah (necessity) justifies emergency slaughter.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Hanafis respond that once the animal comes into the hunter's hand, it ceases to be 'sayd' (game), so the ruling of emergency slaughter (dhakaat al-idadtirari) no longer applies.
The condition for emergency slaughter is that the animal is free and uncaught.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if a hunter catches a live animal but cannot slaughter it in time, Hanafis prohibit eating it; Shafi'is permit it.
However, if the animal cannot survive the wound, even Hanafis permit it via emergency slaughter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شکار میں حیات ہو اور مذبوح کی حیات سے زیادہ ہو ذبح پر قدرت ہونے یا نہ ہونے کا حکم وفصل بعضهم فيه تفصيلا وهو انه ان لم يتمكن لفقد الألة لم يوكل وان لم يتمكن لضيق الوقت لم يوكل عندنا خلا فاللشافعى لانه اذا وقع فى يده لم يبق صيدا فبطل حكم ذكاة الاضطرا روهذا اذا كان يتوهم بقاؤه ترجمہ اور بعض مشائخ نے اس میں ( جبکہ شکار میں مذبوح سے زیادہ حیات ہو) کچھ تفصیل کی ہے اور وہ یہ ہے کہ اگر وہ قادر نہ ہو آلہ کے مفقود ہونے کی وجہ سے تو نہیں کھایا جائے گا اور اگر قادر نہ ہو وقت کی تنگی کی وجہ سے تو ہمارے نزدیک نہیں کھایا جائے گا اختلاف ہے امام شافعی کا (ہماری دلیل یہ ہے ) اس لئے کہ شکار جب اس کے ہاتھ میں آ گیا تو شکار نہیں رہا تو ذکاۃ اضطراری کا حکم باطل ہو گیا اور یہ ( عدم حلت ) جب ہے جب کہ اس کی بقاء کا تو ہم ہو۔ تشریح بعض مشائخ نے اس کی یہ تفصیل بیان کی ہے کہ اگر شکار میں حیات ہے اور وہ حیات مذبوح کی حیات سے زیادہ ہے اس دیکھا جائے کہ ذبح پر قدرت کیوں نہیں اگر چھری وغیرہ نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے قدرت نہیں تو اس کا کھانا حلال نہ ہو گا اس لئے کہ کوتاہی شکاری کی طرف سے ہے کہ اس نے چھری کو کیوں ساتھ نہیں رکھا۔ اور اگر قدرت نہ ہونے کا سبب وقت کی تنگی ہے تو بھی ہمارے نزدیک نہیں کھایا جائے گا امام شافعی کے نزدیک وہ حلال ہے اور یہ استحسان ہے اور یہی حسن بن زیاد اور محمد بن مقاتل کا قول ہے اور قاضی خان نے اس کو اختیار کیا ہے ان حضرات کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ جب وقت کی تنگی کی وجہ سے اصل پر قدرت نہ ہوسکی تو ذکا و اضطراری حلت کا سبب موجود ہے۔ ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ جب شکار شکاری کے ہاتھ میں آ گیا تو اب وہ شکار نہیں رہا تو اب ذکاۃ اضطراری کا حکم باطل ہو گیا کیونکہ ذكاة اضطراری صید میں چلتی ہے اور اب وہ صید نہیں ہے مگر یہ تفصیل اور اس کا حلال نہ ہونا بغیر ذبح کے اس وقت ہے جبکہ اس بات کا تو ہم ہو کہ وہ شکار زخم کے ساتھ زندہ رہ سکتا ہے ورنہ اگر کیفیت یہ ہو کہ وہ اس زخم کے ساتھ زندہ نہیں رہ سکتا تو ذکاو اضطراری کافی ہو جائے گی (فتا مل )
If the game has life and that life is more than that of a slaughtered animal, the ruling on having or not having ability to slaughter: Some scholars detailed it, stating that if he is unable due to lack of a tool, it will not be eaten. And if he is unable due to time constraint, according to us, it will not be eaten; there is disagreement with al-Shafi'i. Because when it comes into his hand, it is no longer game, so the ruling of emergency slaughter becomes void. This applies when its survival is expected. Translation: Some scholars have detailed this (when the game has more life than a slaughtered animal): If he is unable due to lack of a tool, it will not be eaten. If he is unable due to time constraint, according to us it will not be eaten; there is disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i. (Our proof is) because when the game came into his hand, it is no longer game, so the ruling of emergency slaughter becomes void. And this (non-permissibility) applies when its survival is expected. Commentary: Some scholars have detailed that if the game has life and that life is more than that of a slaughtered animal, it is considered whether inability to slaughter is due to lacking a tool (like a knife) — then it is not halal because the hunter was negligent. If the cause is time constraint, then according to us it is also not eaten. According to al-Shafi'i, it is halal, and this is istihsan, and it is also the saying of Hasan ibn Ziyad and Muhammad ibn Muqatil, and Qadi Khan adopted it. Their proof: when due to time constraint he could not perform the primary (slaughter), the cause for emergency slaughter exists. Our proof: when the game came into the hunter's hand, it is no longer game, so the ruling of emergency slaughter is void, because emergency slaughter applies to game, and it is no longer game. But this detail and its non-permissibility without slaughter applies when it is expected that the game can survive with that wound. Otherwise, if it cannot survive with that wound, then emergency slaughter will suffice (fatwa).
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with an intra-Hanafi and inter-madhhab dispute regarding a hunted animal that retains more life than a slaughtered animal. Some scholars detail two scenarios: (1) inability to slaughter due to lack of a tool — the animal is not halal, as the hunter is at fault; (2) inability due to time constraint — the Hanafi ruling is that it remains not halal, contrary to al-Shafi'i who deems it halal. The Hanafi proof is that once the animal comes into the hunter's possession, it ceases to be 'game' (صيد), thereby invalidating the emergency slaughter (ذكاة اضطراري). However, if the animal is so severely wounded that survival is impossible, then the emergency slaughter suffices. The passage also notes that some later Hanafi authorities (Hasan ibn Ziyad, Muhammad ibn Muqatil, Qadi Khan) adopt the Shafi'i view as istihsan.

Key Terms
صید (sayd): Game animal that is free and not in the hunter's possession.
ذکاۃ اضطراری (dhakaat al-idadtirari): Emergency slaughter by wounding that is permissible only for game that cannot be caught alive for proper slaughter.
قدرت (qudrah): Ability to perform the slaughter; absence due to tool or time affects the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is halal because emergency slaughter applies.
B) It is not halal because the animal is no longer game.
C) It is halal only if the hunter had a tool.
D) It is halal only if the animal cannot survive.

Key Takeaway
A game animal with more life than a slaughtered animal is not halal without proper slaughter once caught, even if unable to slaughter due to time constraint, according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Animal with Belly Torn by Trained Dog
Intra-Hanafi dispute on animal with entrails removed by dog

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the permissibility of a game animal whose belly is torn open by a trained dog, and identify the key analogy used by each.

Core Idea
The Sahibayn permit without slaughter; Abu Hanifa requires it – life expectancy determines the ruling.

Within the Hanafi school, there is a notable difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two foremost disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), regarding a game animal that has its belly torn open by a trained dog and its entrails removed. The central question is whether such an animal, found alive but with fatal injuries, requires proper slaughter before consumption. This lesson unpacks the proofs, analogies, and the practical outcome of this internal ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of dispute
Recognize that the case involves a game animal whose belly has been torn open by a trained dog, entrails removed, and the hunter finds it alive. The dispute is whether it is halal without further slaughter.
The animal is clearly injured beyond survival, but it still shows signs of life (movement).
2
State the Sahibayn's position and their proof
The Sahibayn rule that the animal is halal without additional slaughter. Their proof is that the remaining movement is not genuine life but the convulsion (iḍṭirāb) of a slaughtered animal, analogous to a goat that falls into water after being slaughtered.
They consider the movement akin to post-slaughter muscle spasms, which do not indicate life.
3
State Abu Hanifa's position and his proof
Abu Hanifa rules that the animal is not halal without proper slaughter. His proof is that the animal came into the hunter's hand alive, so it must be slaughtered, analogizing to the case of mutaraddiyah (an animal that falls from a height) which requires slaughter if found alive.
He treats any sign of life (even convulsive) as sufficient to require formal slaughter.
4
Recognize the key distinction
The core issue is whether the observed movement constitutes genuine life (ḥayāh ḥaqīqiyyah) or merely a reflex (ḥarakat al-madhbūḥ). The Sahibayn view it as reflex; Abu Hanifa as life.
This distinction determines the need for slaughter.
5
Note the mufta bihi according to later authorities
Later Hanafi authorities, such as Shaykh Abu Bakr al-Jassas al-Razi, state that the Sahibayn's view is recorded in the Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah and is the relied-upon position for fatwā.
Thus in practice, the animal is considered halal without further slaughter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شکاری کتے نے شکار کا پیٹ پھاڑ دیا اور جو کچھ اس کے پیٹ میں ہے نکال دیا پھر مالک کے ہاتھ آ گیا تو یہ شکار حلال ہے اما اذا شـق بـطـنـه واخرج ما فيه ثم وقع فى يدصاحبه حل لانه مابقى اضطراب المذبوح فلا يعتبر كما اذا وقعت شاة فى الماء بعد ما ذبحت وقيل لهذا قولهما اما عند ابي حنيفة لا يوكل ايضا لانه وقع في يده حيا فلا يحل الا بذكاة الاختيار ردا الى المتردية على ما نذكره ان شاء الله تعالى ترجمہ بہر حال جبکہ کلب معلم نے اس کا پیٹ پھاڑ دیا اور جو اس کے پیٹ میں ہے اس کو نکال دیا پھر اس کے مالک کے ہاتھ میں آگیا تو حلال ہے اس لئے کہ جو چیز باقی ہے وہ مذبوح کا اضطراب ہے تو اس کا اعتبار نہیں کیا جائے گا جیسا کہ جب بکری پانی میں گر گئی بعد اس کے وہ کو ذبح کر دیا گیا ہے اور کہا گیا ہے کہ یہ صاحبین کا قول ہے بہر حال ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک یہ بھی نہیں کھایا جائے گا۔ اس لئے کہ شکار اس کے ہاتھ میں زندہ آگیا پس وہ حلال نہیں ہو گا زکاۃ اختیاری کے ساتھ متردیہ پر قیاس کرتے ہوئے جس کو ہم انشاء اللہ بیان کریں گے۔ تشریح ماقبل میں کہا گیا ہے کہ بغیر ذبح کے عدم حلت اس وقت ہے جبکہ اس کی حیات کا تو ہم ہو اور اگر تو ہم نہ ہوتو وہ حلال ہے لہذا فرمایا کہ اگر معلم کتے نے شکار کا پیٹ پھاڑا اس کی آنت اوجھ باہر نکال دیا تو اسکی حیات غیر متوہم ہے اگر شکاری نے اس شکار کو زندہ پالیا تب بھی بغیر ذبح کے حلال ہے اس لئے کہ اس میں جو حیات محسوس ہو رہی ہے وہ حیات نہیں بلکہ مذبوح کی پھڑک ہے جس کا کوئی اعتبار نہیں ہے جیسے اگر ذبح کے بعد بکری پانی میں گر جائے تو کوئی حرج نہیں ہے اس پر شیخ ابو بکر جصاص رازی فرماتے ہیں کہ یہ حلت کا حکم صرف صاحبین کا قول ہے۔ اور امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک تو مشقوق البطن بھی حلال نہ ہوگا کیونکہ جب وہ مالک کو زندہ مل گیا تو اس کی حلت ذکاۃ اختیاری کے بغیر نہ ہوگی ۔ اور یہ متردیہ کے مثل ہے یعنی وہ جانور جو کنویں میں گر جائے یا پہاڑ یا چھت سے لڑھک جائے تو وہاں بھی یہی حکم ہے کہ اگر زندہ مل جائے اور ذبح کر دیا جائے تو حلال ہے ورنہ نہیں یہی مشقوق البطن کا حال ہے۔
If a trained dog tears open its belly and removes what is inside, then it comes into the owner's hand, this game is halal. However, when it tears its belly and removes what is inside, then it comes into its owner's hand, it is halal because whatever remains is the convulsion of a slaughtered animal, so it is not considered, just as when a goat falls into water after it has been slaughtered. And it is said this is their (the two's) statement. But according to Abu Hanifa, it will not be eaten either, because it came into his hand alive, so it is not halal except with a proper slaughter by choice, analogizing to the mutaraddiyah (animal that falls), as we will mention if Allah wills. Translation: In any case, if a trained dog tears its belly and removes what is in its belly, then it comes into the hand of its owner, it is halal because what remains is the convulsion of a slaughtered animal, so it is not considered, just as when a goat falls into water after it has been slaughtered. And it is said that this is the statement of the two (Sahibayn). In any case, according to Abu Hanifa, this also will not be eaten, because the game came into his hand alive, so it will not be halal except with a slaughter by choice, analogizing to the mutaraddiyah, which we will mention if Allah wills. Commentary: It was stated before that non-permissibility without slaughter applies when its life is expected; if its life is not expected, it is halal. So it is said that if a trained dog tears the game's belly and removes its intestines, its life is not expected. Even if the hunter finds it alive, it is halal without slaughter because the life perceived is not real life but the convulsion of a slaughtered animal, which is of no consequence. Just as if a goat falls into water after being slaughtered, no harm. On this, Shaykh Abu Bakr al-Jassas al-Razi says that this ruling of permissibility is only the statement of the two (Sahibayn). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the animal with torn belly is not halal because when it is found alive by the owner, its permissibility cannot be without a proper slaughter. And it is like the mutaraddiyah (animal that falls), i.e., an animal that falls into a well, or rolls off a mountain or roof. There too the ruling is that if it is found alive and slaughtered, it is halal; otherwise not. This is the case of the animal with torn belly.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding an animal whose belly is torn open by a trained dog (کلب معلم) and its entrails are removed. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) rule that such an animal is halal without further slaughter because the remaining movement is merely the convulsion of a slaughtered animal, analogous to a goat that falls into water after being slaughtered. Imam Abu Hanifa, however, rules that it is not halal because the animal comes into the hunter's hand alive, thus requiring a proper slaughter by choice (ذکاة اختیاري). He draws an analogy to the case of mutaraddiyah (متردیہ) — an animal that falls from a height — which requires proper slaughter if found alive. Shaykh Abu Bakr al-Jassas confirms that the majority view (Sahibayn) is permissibility, but Abu Hanifa's position is based on the necessity of proper slaughter for any animal found alive in one's possession.

Key Terms
کلب معلم (kalb mu'allam): A trained dog that is taught to hunt and responds to commands.
متردیہ (mutaraddiyah): An animal that has fallen from a height (e.g., into a well, off a cliff) and may be injured or dead.
صاحبین (Sahibayn): The two main disciples of Abu Hanifa: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is halal without additional slaughter.
B) It is not halal without proper slaughter.
C) It is halal only if the dog is not trained.
D) It is halal only if the animal falls into water.

Key Takeaway
For an animal with fatal injuries from a trained dog, the later Hanafi position is permissibility without slaughter, but the dispute shows the importance of the underlying analogy regarding the nature of life signs.

Ruling on Slaughtering Prey Even if Its Belly is Torn
States the ruling that a slaughtered prey with a torn belly is halal by Hanafi consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on slaughtering a prey animal with a torn belly and distinguish the legal bases of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Slaughtering a torn-belly prey makes it halal by Hanafi consensus, despite different legal grounds.

The passage addresses a common hunting scenario: a prey animal is attacked and its belly torn open. Some may think it is automatically haram (dead from injury). However, the Hanafi school rules that if the hunter reaches it alive and slaughters it, it becomes halal by consensus, though the reasoning differs between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn. This lesson unpacks that ruling and the legal terminology involved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is 'slaughtering a prey animal that has a torn belly'. The ruling is that the meat becomes halal (permissible to eat).
2
Understand the context
The passage clarifies that the earlier discussion of impermissibility only applied when the hunter left the animal without slaughtering. Here, he slaughters it, so the ruling changes.
This highlights the principle that slaughter (tadhkiyah) overrides previous injury.
3
Distinguish the two bases
Abu Hanifa holds that voluntary slaughter (dhakat ikhtiyari) renders it halal. The Sahibayn hold that emergency slaughter (dhakat idtirari) was already sufficient. Both agree on the outcome.
The difference is only in the legal reasoning (thubut al-hukm), not the practical ruling (mufta bihi).
4
Recognize the consensus
Despite the different bases, the practical ruling is unanimous (muttafaq alayh) among the Hanafi imams.
This consensus (ittifaq) is noted in the text.
5
Apply the terminology
Use the terms dhakat ikhtiyari (voluntary slaughter) and dhakat idtirari (emergency slaughter) correctly when explaining the ruling.
Dhakat ikhtiyari requires a deliberate act of slaughter; dhakat idtirari applies when the hunter's wounding inevitably causes death.

Source Text

Book of Hunting. Page 134. Ashraf al-Bidayah Sharh Urdu Hidayah Vol 14. If the prey is slaughtered, even if it is with a torn belly, then this prey is halal by consensus. This which we have mentioned: when he leaves the slaughter, then if he slaughters it, its eating is halal according to Abu Hanifa. Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Book of Hunting. It states that if a hunter simply slaughters a prey animal, even if the animal's belly has been torn open (e.g., from a predator's attack), the meat becomes halal by consensus of the Hanafi imams. The Arabic text clarifies that the earlier discussion of impermissibility only applied when the hunter left the animal without slaughtering it. Here, the act of slaughtering in the normal manner (dhakat ikhtiyari) makes the meat halal according to Abu Hanifa. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) also agree that it is halal, but their basis is that the emergency slaughter (dhakat idtirari) was already sufficient due to the animal's condition. Despite the difference in legal reasoning, the practical ruling (fatwa) is that slaughtering the prey renders it halal regardless of the extent of injury.

Key Terms
ذكاة اختیاری (dhakat ikhtiyari): Voluntary slaughter performed by the hunter in the normal manner by cutting the throat.
مشقوق البطن (mashquq al-batn): An animal whose belly has been torn open, typically by a predator or fall.
ذكاة اضطراری (dhakat idtirari): Emergency slaughter; the hunter's wounding that causes death when voluntary slaughter is not possible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is haram unconditionally
B) It is halal by consensus among Hanafi imams
C) It is halal only according to Abu Hanifa
D) It is halal only if the animal still has visible life

Key Takeaway
Slaughtering a prey animal with a torn belly renders its meat halal by Hanafi consensus, regardless of the injury, provided the animal was alive at slaughter.

Condition of Life in Injured Prey: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on murtadiyyah, natiḥah, mawqudhah, wolf-torn prey with life condition dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the required level of life (hayat) for slaughtered prey and apply the mufta bihi ruling to cases of hidden vs. apparent life.

Core Idea
For Abu Hanifa, any life suffices; the Sahibayn require apparent life for valid slaughter.

This lesson examines the intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding the condition of life in prey animals that have suffered severe injuries. The Quranic verse 'except what you slaughter' is interpreted differently by Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding this ikhtilaf is crucial for determining when such prey becomes halal through slaughter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories of injured prey
Name murtadiyyah (fallen), natiḥah (gored), mawqudhah (beaten), and wolf-torn prey. These are animals that would be haram if found dead because they died from causes other than proper slaughter.
These categories are explicitly mentioned in the passage.
2
State the common issue
The issue is whether the presence of any life (even hidden) at the time of slaughter makes the meat halal, or only apparent life.
The dispute hinges on the definition of sufficient life.
3
Examine Abu Hanifa's position and dalil
Abu Hanifa holds that any life suffices, citing Quran 5:3 as an absolute exception.
The verse 'illa mā dhakkaytum' is interpreted without distinction between types of life.
4
Examine the Sahibayn's position and their divergence
They require apparent life. Abu Yusuf defines it as life sufficient for survival; Muhammad defines it as life exceeding that of a normally slaughtered animal.
This intra-Sahibayn ikhtilaf affects borderline cases.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The text states the fatwa is on Abu Hanifa's view, making any life sufficient in the operative ruling.
Tarjih is given to Abu Hanifa despite the Sahibayn's valid arguments.

كتاب الصيد

امام ابو یوسف کا نقطہ نظر
و عندابي يوسف اذا كان بحال لا يعيش مثلاً لا يحل لانه لم يكن موته بالذبح قال محمد ان كان يعيش مثله فوق ما يعيش المذبوح يحل والا فلا لانه لا معتبر بهذه الحيوة على ما قررناه
ترجمہ اور ابو یوسف کے نزدیک جبکہ و شکار ایسے حال پر ہو کہ اس کے مثل زندہ نہیں رہ سکتا تو وہ حلال نہیں ہے اس لئے اس کا مرنا ذبح کی وجہ سے نہیں ہے اور فرمایا محمد نے اگر اس کے مثل زندہ رہ سکتا نہ بوح کے زندہ رہنے سے زیادہ تو حلال ہے ورنہ نہیں اس لئے کہ اس حیات کا کوئی اعتبار نہیں ہے اسی تفصیل کے مطابق جس کو ہم بیان کر چکے ہیں۔
تنبیہ مطلق شکار میں اور متردیہ وغیرہ میں مطلق حیات معتبر ہے ملاحظہ ہو شامی ص ۳۰۳ ج ۵-
Murtadiyyah, natiḥah, mawqudhah, and the prey whose belly a wolf has torn: if there is life in it, whether hidden or apparent, and if it is slaughtered, then it is halal; otherwise haram. And likewise murtadiyyah, natiḥah, mawqudhah, and that which the wolf tore its belly, and in it is hidden or apparent life, and upon it is the fatwa, due to the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'except what you slaughter' — He exempted it absolutely without distinction. Translation: And similarly murtadiyyah, natiḥah, and mawqudhah, and that which the wolf tore its belly, and in it is hidden or apparent life, and upon this is the fatwa due to Allah's command 'except what you slaughter' — Allah exempted it absolutely without any detail. Commentary: Murtadiyyah is that which falls from a height and dies; natiḥah is that which dies from another animal's horns; mawqudhah is that which is struck with sticks and dies. Apparent life is the apparent life that exceeds the life of a slaughtered animal. Hidden life is the life from which only the estimation that it is alive can be made. When this is understood, then listen: the author says that whether it is murtadiyyah, natiḥah, mawqudhah, or that which the wolf has torn its belly, and there is life in it, whether hidden or apparent, then if in that state they are slaughtered, they are halal; otherwise haram. And emergency slaughter is completely absent here, and the fatwa is on the saying of Imam Abu Hanifa. And Imam Abu Hanifa deduced from this verse in which He said 'except what you slaughter' — so from the prohibited things, the exemption of slaughter is given absolutely, in which there is no distinction between apparent and hidden life. According to the Sahibayn, if there is apparent life, then slaughtering it is valid; otherwise haram. Then between the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) there is a difference as to what is apparent life: Imam Abu Yusuf said that if there is enough life that it could survive, then it is apparent life; otherwise hidden. So in the second case, if it was slaughtered, that slaughter would not be valid because its death is not from the slaughter. And Imam Muhammad says that if there is as much life as a slaughtered animal has, then it is hidden life which is invalid; and if it exceeds the life of a slaughtered animal, then it is apparent life which is valid, and slaughtering it is valid and the animal becomes halal. The author says this. The viewpoint of Imam Abu Yusuf: 'And according to Abu Yusuf, if it is in a state where it cannot live like it, then it is not halal because its death is not from slaughter. Muhammad said: if it can live like it, above what a slaughtered animal lives, then it is halal; otherwise not, because this life is not considered, according to what we have established.' Translation: And according to Abu Yusuf, when the prey is in such a state that it cannot survive like it, then it is not halal because its death is not due to slaughter. And Muhammad said: if it can survive like it, more than the survival of a slaughtered animal, then it is halal; otherwise not, because this life has no consideration, according to the detail we have explained. Note: In absolute hunting and in murtadiyyah etc., absolute life is valid. See Shami p. 303 vol. 5.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Hunting deals with the ruling on prey animals that have been seriously injured but are still alive. It discusses four specific categories: (1) al-murtadiyyah: an animal that falls from a height and dies; (2) al-natiḥah: an animal gored to death by another animal's horns; (3) al-mawqudhah: an animal beaten to death with sticks; and (4) an animal whose belly has been torn by a wolf. The passage first states the ruling: if any of these animals is found with life in it — whether that life is hidden (khafiyyah, meaning only a slight sign of life) or apparent (zāhirah, meaning a stronger life force) — and the hunter slaughters it in that state, the meat is halal. If it is not slaughtered, it is haram. The author then notes that the fatwa is on this view, citing the Quranic verse (5:3): 'except what you slaughter' (illa mā dhakkaytum). Imam Abu Hanifa argues that this verse is an absolute exception that does not differentiate between hidden and apparent life; any life suffices. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree, holding that only apparent life makes the slaughter valid. They then differ among themselves on the definition of apparent life: Abu Yusuf says it is the ability to survive; Muhammad says it is life exceeding that of a normally slaughtered animal. The passage concludes with a note that in all these cases, absolute life is considered, citing al-Shami.

Key Terms
متردیة (murtadiyyah): An animal that falls from a height and dies as a result.
نطیحة (natiḥah): An animal that is gored to death by another animal's horns.
موقوذة (mawqudhah): An animal that is beaten to death with sticks or clubs.
حیات ظاہرہ (hayat zahirah): Apparent life that is stronger than the life of a normally slaughtered animal, indicating the animal could survive for some time.
حیات خفیہ (hayat khafiyyah): Hidden life that only indicates the animal is still alive but may not be sufficient for sustained survival.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, upon whose opinion is the fatwa (operative ruling) in the Hanafi school?
A) Imam Abu Hanifa
B) Imam Abu Yusuf
C) Imam Muhammad
D) Sahibayn both

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, for injured prey (murtadiyyah, natiḥah, mawqudhah, wolf-torn), the mufta bihi is that any life at the time of slaughter makes the meat halal, following Imam Abu Hanifa.

Ruling on Finding Prey Without Catching It
Ruling on prey found but not caught: if capture and slaughter possible, not halal; otherwise halal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a hunter who finds prey without catching it, and distinguish between when it is halal and when it is not based on the hunter's ability to slaughter.

Core Idea
Prey found but not caught is halal only if the hunter could not have caught and slaughtered it.

In the previous lesson, we learned the ruling when a hunter catches prey. Now we consider a scenario where the hunter sees the prey but does not physically capture it. The key distinction is whether the hunter realistically had the ability to capture and slaughter the animal at that moment. This lesson explains the legal consequence of that ability (or lack thereof).

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The hunter finds prey but does not catch it. This is different from capturing it.
2
Check the time factor
Was there enough time for the hunter to catch and slaughter the prey? This determines the ruling.
Time refers to the realistic opportunity, not mere possibility.
3
Determine the ruling
If the hunter had the ability to catch and slaughter, the meat is not halal. If he did not, it is halal.
The animal is considered 'under his power' (maqdūr ʿalayh) in the first case.
4
Understand the reasoning
Mere finding does not establish possession or ability to slaughter. Only actual ability to capture and slaughter makes the animal unlawful if not slaughtered.
This contrasts with the case where the hunter catches the prey.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اگر شکاری نے شکار کو پکڑا نہیں اور اسے پالیا اور اتنا وقت تھا کہ وہ اسے پکڑ کر ذبح کر سکتا تھا لیکن اس نے ذبح نہیں کیا تو کھانا حلال نہیں
ولو ادركه ولم ياخذه فان كان في وقت لو اخذه امكنه ذبحه لم يوكل لانه صار فى حكم المقدور عليه وان کان لایــمــکــنـــه ذبحه اكل لان اليد لم تثبت به والتمكن من الذبح لم يوجد
ترجمہ اور اگر شکاری نے شکار کو پا لیا اور اس کو پکڑا نہیں پس اگر اتنا وقت ہو کہ اگر وہ اس کو پکڑتا تو اس کو اس کا ذبح کرنا ممکن ہوتا تو نہیں کھایا جائے گا اس لئے کہ وہ مقدور علیہ کے معنی میں ہو گیا اور اگر اس کو اس کا ذبح کرنا ممکن نہ ہوتا تو کھایا جائے گا اس لئے کہ پانے سے قبضہ ثابت نہیں ہوا اور ذبح کی قدرت نہیں پائی گئی۔
تشریح پہلے مسائل میں شکاری نے شکار کو پکڑ لیا تھا اور یہاں پکڑ انہیں اگر چہ اس کو پالیا ہے تو اگر اتنا وقت ہو کہ شکاری اتنے وقت میں شکار کو پکڑ سکتا ہے اور ذبح کر سکتا ہے لیکن اس نے نہیں کیا تو اس کا کھانا حلال نہیں ہے کیونکہ اس پر ذبح کی قدرت حاصل ہے اس کو مصنف نے مقدور علیہ سے تعبیر فرمایا ہے اور اگر اتنا وقت نہیں ہے تو پھر کھانا حلال ہے اس لئے کہ محض پالینے سے بغیر اخذ کے قبضہ ثابت نہیں ہوتا لہذا ذبح کی قدرت بھی نہیں پائی گئی اس وجہ سے اس کا کھانا حلال ہے۔
If the hunter did not catch the prey and found it, and there was time such that he could have caught and slaughtered it, but he did not slaughter it, then it is not halal to eat. And if he found it and did not take it, then if it was in a time such that if he took it, he could have slaughtered it, it is not eaten because it came under the ruling of that which is under his power. And if he could not slaughter it, then it is eaten because the hand was not established over it and the ability to slaughter was not found. Translation: And if the hunter found the prey and did not catch it, then if there is enough time that if he caught it, slaughtering it would be possible, then it will not be eaten because it became in the meaning of that which is within his power. And if slaughtering it is not possible, then it will be eaten because mere finding does not establish possession and the ability to slaughter was not found. Commentary: In earlier issues, the hunter caught the prey; here he does not catch it although he found it. So if there is enough time that the hunter could catch and slaughter it in that time but he did not, then eating it is not halal because he had the ability to slaughter it, which the author expressed as 'under his power' (maqdūr ʿalayh). And if there is not enough time, then eating it is halal because mere finding without taking does not establish possession, so the ability to slaughter is also not found; therefore eating it is halal.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage presents a second scenario in the chapter of hunting: the hunter sees the prey but does not physically capture it. The ruling depends on whether the hunter realistically had the ability to capture and slaughter it at that moment. If there was sufficient time for him to catch and slaughter the animal but he chose not to, then the animal is considered to be under his power (maqdūr ʿalayh), and its meat is not halal for him to eat because he failed to slaughter it when he could. If, however, the prey was beyond his reach or he could not catch it in time, then the animal does not fall under his control, and if it dies later or he eats it, it is halal. The reasoning is that mere visual contact (idrāk) does not establish possession (yad) or ability (tamakkun) to slaughter.

Key Terms
مقدور علیہ (maqdūr ʿalayh): Something that is within one's power or control; here, the prey is considered to be under the hunter's power if he could have caught and slaughtered it with reasonable effort.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a hunter sees a wounded prey but does not catch it, and there was enough time to catch and slaughter it, but he does not do so, what is the ruling?
A) It is halal because he saw it
B) It is not halal because it is under his power
C) It is halal only if the animal has stable life
D) It is not halal only if the animal dies immediately

Key Takeaway
The halal status of prey found but not caught depends on whether the hunter had the realistic ability to catch and slaughter it.

Finding and Slaughtering Prey: Stable Life and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling with proof by ijma and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a found prey that is slaughtered, distinguish between the presence and absence of stable life, and explain the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Stable life triggers ijma; without it, Abu Hanifa still requires slaughter while the Sahibayn do not.

When a hunter finds and slaughters his prey, the ruling depends on whether the animal had stable life (hayat mustaqirrah) at the time of slaughter. If it did, the meat is halal by consensus. If it did not, the leading Hanafi authorities differ: Imam Abu Hanifa insists on slaughter for halal, while Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad consider it unnecessary. This lesson unpacks that difference and its practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage divides the case into two: (1) the prey has stable life at the moment of slaughter, and (2) it does not. Identify what 'stable life' means and why it matters.
Stable life means the animal would survive if left alone.
2
State the ijma ruling for stable life
When stable life is present, the slaughter is effective by consensus (bil-ijma). The meat is halal without any ikhtilaf.
3
Contrast the views on unstable life
When stable life is absent, Abu Hanifa holds that slaughter (dhabh) still renders the meat halal. The Sahibayn hold that slaughter is not needed because the animal is effectively dead.
Note: This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: According to Abu Hanifa, the hunter must slaughter the animal even if it appears dead; according to the Sahibayn, he may eat it without slaughtering.
5
Recognise the textbook limitation
The passage is cut off and does not state the tarjih (preferred view), but earlier passages indicate the fatwa is on Abu Hanifa's position.
Refer to Lesson 2 of this chapter for the tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اگر پالیا اور ذبح کر لیا تو حلال ہے
وان ادر كه فذكاه حل له لانه ان كانت فيه حيوة مستقرة فالذكاة وقعت موقعها بالاجماع وان لم تكن فيه حلوة مستقرة فعندابي حنيفة ذكاته الذبح على ما ذكرناه وقد وجد وعندهما لا يحتاج الى الذبح
ترجمہ اور اگر شکاری نے شکار کو پا لیا پس اس کو ذبح کر دیا تو وہ اس کے لئے حلال ہے اس لئے کہ اگر اس میں حیات مستعقرہ ہوگی تو ذبح کرنا اپنے مقام پر واقع ہو گیا ہے بالا جماع اور اگر اس میں حیات متعقرہ ن ہوگی تو ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک اس کی ذکاۃ ذبح کرنا ہے اس
If he found it and slaughtered it, then it is halal. And if he found it and slaughtered it, it is halal for him, because if there is stable life in it, then the slaughter has fallen in its place by consensus. And if there is no stable life in it, then according to Abu Hanifa, its slaughter (dhakat) is the slaughter (dhabh) as we have mentioned, and it has occurred; and according to the two (Sahibayn), it does not need slaughter. Translation: And if the hunter found the prey and then slaughtered it, then it is halal for him, because if there is stable life in it, the slaughter has fallen in its place by consensus. And if there is no stable life in it, then according to Abu Hanifa, its slaughter is the slaughter (dhabh) as we have mentioned, and it has occurred; and according to the two, it does not need slaughter.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling when the hunter finds the prey and actually slaughters it. The text begins by stating that it is halal. It then provides the proof: if the animal has stable life (hayāt mustaqirrah) at the time of slaughter, the slaughter is effective by consensus (bil-ijmāʿ). If the animal does not have stable life (i.e., it is essentially dead or dying), then the ruling differs: according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the slaughter still counts as its legal slaughter (dhakāh) and makes it halal, because he considers the act of slaughter to be valid even if the animal had only faint life (as established earlier). According to the Sahibayn, in such a case the meat is already halal without slaughter because the animal's death is due to the injury, and no further slaughter is needed. The text is cut off but likely continues to discuss the tarjih.

Key Terms
حیات مستقرہ (hayat mustaqirrah): Stable life; life that is firmly present and would sustain the animal if not slaughtered.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling when a found prey has stable life and is slaughtered?
A) It is halal by ijma'
B) It is halal only according to Abu Hanifa
C) It is halal only according to the Sahibayn
D) It is haram

Key Takeaway
When stable life is present, slaughter is effective by ijma; when absent, Abu Hanifa requires slaughter while the Sahibayn do not, and the mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Slaughter of Game Caught by a Dog
Intra-Hanafi dispute on slaughter condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the requirement of slaughter for game caught by a trained dog, and identify when consensus applies.

Core Idea
Slaughter is required for game caught by a dog per Abu Hanifa, but not per Sahibayn; however, if slaughtered, it is halal by consensus.

This passage delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the necessity of slaughter after a hunting dog catches game. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that slaughter is a condition for halal consumption even if the animal appears lifeless (hayat khafiyyah), while the Sahibayn argue that if the animal is dead from the dog's attack, no slaughter is needed. The passage then explains that if slaughter is performed, it becomes halal by consensus, whether the animal had no life or had stable life (hayat mustaqirrah). Understanding this dispute clarifies the conditions under which game is considered halal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed ruling
What specific issue is being debated? The passage concerns the requirement of slaughter for game caught by a trained hunting dog.
The dispute is not about the dog's training, but about the necessity of additional slaughter after the dog has killed the game.
2
State the position of the Sahibayn
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, if the animal is dead from the dog's attack, no further slaughter is required for it to be halal.
They consider the dog's killing as sufficient if the dog is trained and the animal is dead.
3
State the position of Abu Hanifa
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that slaughter is a condition (shart) for halal consumption, even if the animal appears dead and only has hidden life (hayat khafiyyah).
He requires that the hunter perform slaughter to ensure the blood is drained, distinguishing between hidden life and stable life.
4
Recognize the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if the animal is retrieved dead, Sahibayn allow eating without slaughter; Abu Hanifa prohibits unless slaughter is done, even if no signs of life.
This can affect the hunter's practice depending on which view is followed.
5
Identify the consensus scenario
If the hunter does slaughter the animal, it becomes halal by consensus, regardless of whether the animal had no life or had stable life.
The consensus applies because slaughter is complete and valid in both cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تفصیل کے مطابق جس کو ہم ذکر کر چکے ہیں اور ذبح کرنا پایا گیا ہے اور صاحبین کے نزدیک ذبح کی حاجت نہیں ہے۔ تشریح اور اگر اس نے شکار کو پکڑ کر اس کو ذبح کر دیا تو بالا جماع حلال ہو گیا کیونکہ صاحبین کے نزدیک تو یہاں ذبح کی حالت ہی نہیں جبکہ اس میں حیات نہیں ہے اور ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک ذبح کرنا شرط ہے اگر چہ حیات خفیہ بھی ہو تو شرط پوری ہوگئی اور اس کو کھانا حلال ہو گیا اور اس میں حیات مستعقرہ ہو تو پھر بھی بالا جماع حلال ہو گیا کیونکہ یہ ذبح کامل تھا اور اس کو ذبح کر دیا گیا ہے۔
According to the detail we have already mentioned, slaughter has been found, and according to the two companions (Sahibayn) there is no need for slaughter. Commentary: And if he caught the game and slaughtered it, then by consensus it becomes halal, because according to the two companions, there is no state of slaughter here when there is no life in it; and according to Abu Hanifa, slaughter is a condition, even if life is hidden, the condition is fulfilled and eating it becomes halal. And if it has stable life (hayat mustaqirrah), then also by consensus it becomes halal because this was a complete slaughter and it has been slaughtered.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the necessity of slaughter after a hunting dog catches game. The author first notes that according to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), if the animal is dead from the dog's attack, no further slaughter is required. However, Imam Abu Hanifa holds that slaughter is a condition (shart) for halal consumption, even if the animal appears lifeless (hayat khafiyyah). The commentary explains that if the hunter does slaughter the animal, it becomes halal by consensus in both scenarios: whether the animal had no life or had stable life (hayat mustaqirrah), because the slaughter was complete. The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is generally the view of the Sahibayn, but the text presents both positions.

Key Terms
صاحبین (al-Ṣāḥibayn): The two companions of Abu Hanifa: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani
حیات خفیہ (ḥayāt khafiyyah): Hidden life - the animal appears dead but some life remains
حیات مستعقرہ (ḥayāt mustaqirrah): Stable life - the animal is clearly alive with significant movement

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Slaughter is required for it to be halal

D) It is makruh without slaughter

Key Takeaway
The necessity of slaughter for game caught by a dog is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa requires it, Sahibayn do not if the animal is dead; but if slaughter is performed, it is halal by consensus.

The Ruling When a Hunting Dog Catches Unintended Game
Ruling and inter-madhhab dispute about unintended catch

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a trained dog catches a different animal than intended, explain the Maliki objection, and defend the Hanafi position using rational proof.

Core Idea
A trained dog's catch is halal even if different from the intended game because specification is an ineffective condition.

Hunting with trained dogs is a common practice, but what happens if the dog catches a different animal than the one it was sent after? The Hanafi and Maliki schools disagree on this point. This lesson explores the dispute, the Hanafi proof based on practicality, and the resulting difference in rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Begin by clearly stating: according to the Hanafi school, if a trained dog catches an animal different from the one it was sent after, that animal is halal and permissible to eat.
2
Identify the opposing school and its position
Imam Malik (founder of the Maliki school) holds that such a catch is not halal because the irsal (sending) was specific to the intended game.
The Maliki view: the dog caught without a valid irsal.
3
Follow the Maliki proof
Malik's argument: irsal is only for the intended game (mushār ilayh). Since the dog caught something else, it was not sent after that animal, so the catch is not attributable to the owner's action.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is based on rational reasoning: requiring the dog to catch only the specified game is an ineffective condition. The hunter's objective is obtaining game, and it is impossible to train a dog to exclusively target a specific animal. Therefore, the condition of specification is dropped, and irsal remains valid for any game caught.
The Hanafi proof is aqli (rational), not naqli.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The practical difference: Hanafis permit eating the unintended catch; Malikis prohibit it. This affects the daily practice of hunters who use trained dogs.
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is that it is halal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کسی نے ایک شکار پر کلب معلم چھوڑ اوہ کسی دوسرے جانور کو شکار کر کے لایا تو حلال ہے یا . نہیں، اقوال فقہاء واذا ارسل كلبه على صيد واخذ غيره حل وقال مالك لا يحل لانه اخذ بغیر ارسال اذ الارسال مختص بالمشار اليه ولنا انه شرط غير مفيدلان مقصوده حصول الصيد اذ لا يقدر على الوفاء اذلا يمكنه تعليمه على وجه ياخذ ماعينه فسقط اعتباره ترجمہ اور جب شکاری نے اپنا کتا کسی شکار پر چھوڑا اور اس نے اس کے غیر کو پکڑ لیا تو حلال ہے اور امام مالک نے فرمایا حلال نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ کتے نے بغیر ارسال کے پکڑا ہے اس لئے کہ ارسال مشار الیہ کے ساتھ مختص ہے اور ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہ (معین شکار کو پکڑنا ) ایسی شرط ہے جو غیر مفید ہے اس لئے کہ شکاری کا مقصود شکار کا حصول ہے اس لئے کہ کتا اس شرط کے پورا رنے پر قادر نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ مالک کو ممکن نہیں ہے کتے کو اس طرح تعلیم دینا کہ وہ اس کو پکڑ ہے جس کو اس نے متعین کیا ہے تو اسکا اعتبار ساقط ہو گیا۔ تشریح شکاری نے کسی شکار پر اپنا معلم کتا چھوڑا اور کتے نے دوسرا شکار مارا تو ہمارے نزدیک یہ حلال ہے اور امام مالک کے نزدیک یہ حلال نہیں ہے امام مالک کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ کتے نے بغیر ارسال کے پکڑا ہے کیونکہ ارسال تو فقط اس شکار کا تھا جس کی جانب مالک نے اشارہ کیا ہے۔ اور ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ معین شکار پکڑنے کی شرط لگانا ایسی شرط ہے جو نہ مالک کے لئے مفید ہے اور نہ کتے کے لئے کیونکہ مالک کا مقصود تو شکار ہے نہ کہ معین شکار اور کبھی اگر چہ معین شکار ہی مقصود ہوتا ہے مگر کتا اس کے پورا کرنے سے معذور ہوتا ہے کیونکہ بہر حال کتا کتا ہے یہ غیر ممکن ہے کہ کتے کو ایسی تعلیم دی جائے کہ جس شکار کو مالک نے متعین کر دیا ہے وہ فقط اسی کو پکڑے لہذ ا فقط ارسال معتبر ہوگا تعین معتبر نہ ہوگا۔
Someone released a trained dog on one game, and it brought another animal, is it halal or not? Statements of the jurists. And when he sends his dog on a game and it catches another, it is halal. Malik said: it is not halal because it caught without sending (irsal), since the sending is specific to the intended game. And our proof is that it is a condition that is not beneficial, because his purpose is obtaining game, since (the dog) is not capable of fulfilling (the condition), because it is not possible to teach it in a way that it catches only what he specifies, so its consideration is dropped. Translation: And when a hunter releases his dog on a game and it catches another besides it, it is halal. Imam Malik said: it is not halal because the dog caught without irsal, since irsal is specific to the intended game. Our proof is that this (catching the specified game) is a condition that is not beneficial, because the hunter's purpose is obtaining game, because the dog is not capable of fulfilling this condition, because it is not possible for the owner to teach the dog in a way that it catches only what he specified, so its consideration is dropped. Commentary: A hunter released his trained dog on a game, and the dog killed another game. According to us, it is halal; according to Imam Malik, it is not halal. Imam Malik's proof is that the dog caught without irsal, because irsal was only for the game the owner pointed to. Our proof is that making the condition of catching the specified game is not beneficial for the owner nor for the dog, because the owner's purpose is game, not a specific game; and even if sometimes the specified game is intended, the dog is incapable of fulfilling it because after all a dog is a dog, it is impossible to teach it to catch only what the owner specifies; therefore only irsal is considered, specification is not considered.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage addresses a dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam Malik regarding a dog that catches a different animal than the one it was sent after. The author presents the Hanafi ruling: the animal is halal. Imam Malik argues it is not halal because the dog acted without valid irsal (sending), since the irsal was specific to the intended game. The Hanafi response, based on rational reasoning (dalil aqli), states that requiring the dog to catch only the specified game is an ineffective condition. The hunter's primary objective is to obtain game, and it is practically impossible to train a dog to exclusively catch a designated animal. Therefore, the condition of specification is invalid, and the irsal remains valid for any game the dog catches. The commentary clarifies both positions, emphasizing the Hanafi view that the dog's action is still attributable to the owner's release, and the catch is halal.

Key Terms
ارسال (irsāl): The act of sending a trained hunting dog (or bird) towards game with intention of hunting
مشار الیہ (mushār ilayh): The specific game that the hunter points to or intends when releasing the dog
کلب معلم (kalb muʿallam): A trained hunting dog that responds to commands and catches game without eating it

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling when a hunter releases a trained dog on a specific game and the dog catches a different animal?
A) The catch is halal only if the hunter intended both animals.
B) The catch is halal because the condition of specification is not required.
C) The catch is not halal because the dog disobeyed.
D) The catch is not halal because the irsal was for the intended game.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a trained dog's unintended catch is halal because the condition of specification is impractical and therefore disregarded; only the irsal (sending) matters.

One Basmalah Sufficient for One Act of Sending Dog
Ruling that one tasmiyah suffices for multiple game if the act of sending is one

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that one tasmiyah suffices for multiple game caught by a single irsal and explain the qiyas with simultaneous slaughter.

Core Idea
One tasmiyah suffices for one irsal — the act's unity, not the catch's number, determines the requirement.

When a hunter sends a trained dog after game, the crucial moment is the sending (irsal). This lesson examines whether the hunter must repeat 'Bismillah' for each animal caught or if one suffices. The Hanafi school ties the obligation to the unity of the sending act, not the multiplicity of game, drawing a parallel with simultaneous slaughter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is sending a hunting dog (irsal) on multiple game. The ruling: if the hunter says tasmiyah once at irsal, all game caught by that single sending are halal.
tasmiyah is saying 'Bismillah'.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is qiyas (analogy) with simultaneous slaughter of two sheep. If two sheep are slaughtered in one action, one tasmiyah suffices.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The effective cause (illa) is the unity of the act (wahdat al-fi'l). Just as a single slaughter act unites multiple animals, a single irsal unites multiple game.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
This passage does not discuss disagreement, but other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) consider tasmiyah sunnah rather than a condition. The Hanafi position is that tasmiyah is a condition (shart) at irsal.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The ruling is clearly stated as the operative position (mufta bihi) in the Hidayah, based on the principle that slaughter is realized by the sending itself.

Source Text

ایک فعل ذبح پر ایک بسم اللہ کافی ہے اگر فعل متعدد ہو تو بسم اللہ بھی متعدد ہو ولو ارسله على صيد كثير وسمى مرة واحدة حالة الارسال فلو قتل الكل يحل بهذه التسمية الواحدة لان الذبج يقع بالارسال على مابيناه ولهذا تشترط التسمية عنده والفعل واحد فتكفيه تسمية واحدة بخلاف ذبح الشاتين بتسمية واحدة لان الثانية تصير مذبوحة بفعل غير الأول فلا بد من تسمية اخرى حتى لواضجع احداهما فوق الأخرى وذبحهما بمرة واحدة تحلان بتسمية واحدة ترجمہ اور اگر اس نے بہت سے شکاروں پر کتا چھوڑا اور ایک مرتبہ تسمیہ کہا چھوڑنے کے وقت پس اگر کتے نے تمام کو مار ڈالا تو اسی اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد - ۱۴ ۱۳۷ كتاب الصيد ایک تسمیہ سے کل حلال ہیں اس لئے کہ ذبح کرنا ارسال سے واقع ہو جاتا ہے اس تفصیل کے مطابق جس کو ہم بیان کر چکے ہیں اور اسی وجہ سے ارسال کے وقت تسمیہ شرط ہے اور ارسال ایک فعل ہے تو اس کو ایک تسمیہ کافی ہے بخلاف دو بکریوں کو ذبح کرنے کے ایک تسمیہ سے اس لئے کہ دوسری بکری مذبوح ہوگی ایسے فعل سے جو اول کا غیر ہے تو دوسر تسمیہ ضروری ہے یہاں تک کہ اگر اس نے ایک کو دوسری کے او پر لٹایا اور دونوں کو ایک دم ذبح کر دیا تو یہ دونوں ایک تسمیہ سے حلال ہوں گی۔ تشریح ایک فعل ذبیح پر ایک بسم اللہ کافی ہے اور فعل اگر متعدد ہوں تو بسم اللہ بھی متعدد ہوگی لہذا اگر دو بکریوں کو یکے بعد دیگرے ذبح کرے گا تو فعل میں تعدد ہے تو تسمیہ میں بھی تعدد ہو گا اور اگر دو بکریوں کو ایک دم اوپر نیچے لٹا کر ذبح کیا تو اس کو ایک فعل شمار کیا جائے گا اور ایک تسمیہ کافی ہوگا اسی طرح ارسال کلب بھی ایک فعل ہے تو اس ایک فعل میں تسمیہ بھی ایک ہی ہوگا لہذا اگر ارسال کلب چند شکاروں پر کیا ہو اور کبھی کو اس نے ماردیا ہو تو ایک ہی تسمیہ سے سب حلال ہونگے کیونکہ فعل ارسال ایک ہے تو تسمیہ بھی ایک ہی کافی ہوگا۔
One basmalah is sufficient for one act of slaughter; if the act is multiple, the basmalah is also multiple. And if he sends it on many game and says the tasmiyah once at the time of sending, then if it kills all, they are all halal with this one tasmiyah, because slaughter occurs by the sending as we have explained, and for this reason tasmiyah is a condition at the time of sending, and the act is one, so one tasmiyah suffices for it. Unlike slaughtering two sheep with one tasmiyah, because the second becomes slaughtered by an act other than the first, so another tasmiyah is necessary. Until if he lays one over the other and slaughters both at once, both are halal with one tasmiyah. Translation: And if he released the dog on many game and said tasmiyah once at the time of release, then if the dog killed all, they are all halal with that one tasmiyah, because slaughter occurs by the sending according to the detail we have mentioned, and for this reason tasmiyah is a condition at the time of sending, and the sending is one act, so one tasmiyah is sufficient for it. Unlike slaughtering two sheep with one tasmiyah, because the second sheep is slaughtered by an act different from the first, so another tasmiyah is necessary. If he lays one over the other and slaughters both at once, then both become halal with one tasmiyah. Commentary: One basmalah is sufficient for one act of slaughter; if the acts are multiple, the basmalah will also be multiple. Therefore, if he slaughters two sheep one after the other, there is multiplicity in the act, so there will be multiplicity in the tasmiyah. And if he slaughters two sheep at once by laying one on top of the other, then it is counted as one act, and one tasmiyah is sufficient. Similarly, the sending of the dog is one act, so in this one act, the tasmiyah is also one. Hence, if the sending of the dog is on several game and it kills all of them, they all become halal with one tasmiyah, because the act of sending is one, so one tasmiyah suffices.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage establishes a general principle in Hanafi fiqh: the requirement of saying 'Bismillah' (tasmiyah) is tied to the act, not the number of animals. When hunting with a trained dog, the act is the sending (irsal) of the dog. If the hunter says tasmiyah once at the time of sending, and the dog kills multiple game, all are halal because the act of sending is one. The author draws an analogy with slaughter: if two sheep are slaughtered separately in sequence, each slaughter is a separate act requiring separate tasmiyah. However, if both are slaughtered simultaneously in a single action (e.g., by cutting both throats at once with one knife stroke), it counts as one act, and one tasmiyah suffices. The key distinction is the unity of the action. The passage thus clarifies that multiplication of the tasmiyah does not depend on the number of animals caught, but on the number of independent acts of sending or slaughter. This ruling is based on the principle in the Hidayah that the slaughter (in hunting) is realized by the sending itself, and tasmiyah is required at the time of that single act.

Key Terms
تسمیہ (tasmiyah): Saying 'Bismillah' (in the name of Allah) at the time of sending the dog or slaughtering an animal
ذبح (dhabḥ): The act of slaughtering an animal by cutting the throat

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, when is one tasmiyah sufficient for multiple game caught by a dog?
A) When the dog is sent only on one game but catches multiple
B) When the dog is sent once and catches multiple game
C) When the hunter says tasmiyah after each kill
D) When the dog catches the game one by one

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the requirement of tasmiyah is tied to the unity of the act of sending, not to the number of game caught.

Cheetah Hiding Then Catching Game
Ruling on cheetah hiding as a hunting stratagem

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on game caught by a hunting animal that hides before striking, and distinguish between a pause for strategy and a pause for rest.

Core Idea
A strategic pause by a hunting animal does not break irsal; the catch remains halal.

In Hanafi fiqh, the act of sending (irsal) a trained hunting animal must be continuous for the catch to be attributed to the hunter. This lesson examines a specific scenario: what if the animal, like a cheetah, naturally hides before pouncing? Is the irsal considered broken? The text from Al-Hidayah clarifies that a strategic pause for hunting is not a break, while a rest or deviation is. Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining the permissibility of game.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is sending a cheetah that then hides and catches game. The ruling: it is halal to eat the game.
2
Understand the key concept: irsal
Irsal (sending) must be continuous. Any interruption by the animal for rest or deviation breaks the irsal, making the catch not attributable to the hunter.
The animal's pause is evaluated based on its natural hunting method.
3
Distinguish between stratagem and rest
The text uses 'hilah' (stratagem) for the cheetah's hiding, contrasting it with 'istirahah' (rest). A stratagem is part of the hunting process; rest is not.
The cheetah's natural behavior includes stalking and hiding, so it is considered a stratagem.
4
Apply to dogs
A dog normally does not hide; if it hides without training, it is a break. But if a dog has been trained to behave like a cheetah (i.e., hides as a strategy), the same ruling applies: the pause is a stratagem.
The ruling depends on the animal's habit, not species alone.
5
Extract the principle
The underlying principle: an animal's pause after irsal is evaluated by its purpose. If it is for hunting strategy, irsal continues; if for rest or deviation, irsal is broken.
This principle applies to all trained hunting animals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
چیتا شکار پر چھوڑا گھات لگا کر اس نے شکار کو پکڑا اور اس کو قتل کر دیا تو شکار کا کھانا حلال ہے و من ارسل فهذا فكمن حتى يتمكن ثم اخذ الصيد فقتله يوكل لان مكثه ذلك حيلة منه للصيد لا استراحة فلا يقطع الارسال وكذا الكلب اذا اعتاد عادته ترجمہ اور جس نے چیتا ( شکار پر ) چھوڑ اپس اس نے گھات لگائی یہاں تک کہ وہ قادر ہو جائے پھر اس نے شکار کو پکڑا اپس اس کو قتل کر دیا تو کھایا جائے گا اس لئے کہ اس کا یہ ٹھہرنا اس کی جانب سے شکار کرنے کی تدبیر ہے آرام کرنا نہیں ہے تو یہ ٹھہر نا اس کو منقطع نہیں کرے گا اور ایسے ہی کتا جبکہ اس نے چیتے کی عادت اختیار کر لی ہو۔ تشریح کتنے کی عادت گھات میں بیٹھنے کی نہیں ہوتی لہذا جب اس کو مالک نے چھوڑا اور وہ اپنی سابق روش سے ہٹ گیا اور پھر جا کر شکار مارا تو یہ شکار حلال نہ ہوگا کیونکہ ارسال منقطع ہو گیا ہے۔ اور چیتے کی عادت یہ ہے کہ وہ کسی جگہ چھپ کر داؤ لگاتا ہے اور شکار کو پکڑنے کی تدبیر سوچتا ہے لہذا اگر چیتےکو شکار پر چھوڑا اور وہ کہیں شکار کی تدبیر کے لئے گھات میں بیٹھ گیا اور پھر شکار مارا تو یہ حلال ہے کیونکہ یہاں ارسال منقطع نہیں کیونکہ چیتے کا یہ فعل استراحت نہیں ہے بلکہ شکار کی تدبیر ہے البتہ اگر کسی کتے کی عادت چیتے کی طرح گھات میں بیٹھنے کی ہوگئی ہو تو پھر اس کا حکم بھی وہی ہوگا جو چیتے کا ہے۔
A cheetah released on game, it hid in ambush, then caught and killed the game: eating the game is halal. And whoever releases a cheetah, and it hides until it becomes able, then catches the game and kills it, it is eaten, because its staying there is a stratagem for hunting, not rest, so it does not break the irsal. Similarly, a dog if it adopts that habit. Translation: And whoever releases a cheetah (on game) and it hid in ambush until it becomes able, then caught the game and killed it, then it will be eaten, because its staying there is a stratagem from it for hunting, not rest, so this staying does not break the irsal. And similarly, a dog when it adopts the cheetah's habit. Commentary: A dog's habit is not to sit in ambush; therefore, when the owner releases it and it deviates from its previous way and then goes and kills game, this game will not be halal because the irsal has been broken. And the cheetah's habit is that it hides somewhere and sets a trap and thinks of a plan to catch the game. Therefore, if a cheetah is released on game and it sits in ambush somewhere to plan for the game and then kills, it is halal because here the irsal is not broken, since this action of the cheetah is not rest but a stratagem for hunting. However, if a dog's habit has become like that of a cheetah to sit in ambush, then its ruling will also be the same as that of the cheetah.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage addresses a nuance in hunting law: whether a pause or hiding by the hunting animal breaks the continuity of irsal (the act of sending). The author distinguishes between two types of behavior. A cheetah (fahd) naturally stalks and hides before pouncing; this is a strategic hunting technique (hilah), not a rest (istirahah). Therefore, if the hunter releases a cheetah and it hides before catching the game, the irsal is still considered continuous, and the catch is halal. In contrast, a dog normally does not hide; if a dog disregards the direct chase and stops to rest or deviates, the irsal is broken and the catch is not attributed to the owner's sending. However, if a particular dog has been trained to behave like a cheetah (i.e., to hide and stalk), then it is treated similarly: its pause is considered strategy, not rest, and the catch is halal. The key principle is that the animal's action must be consistent with its training and natural hunting method. If the pause is for hunting strategy, irsal remains valid; if it is for rest or straying, irsal is severed.

Key Terms
ارسال (irsāl): The act of sending a trained hunting animal towards game; it establishes the hunter's ownership of the catch
فهد (fahd): Cheetah
حیلہ (hīlah): Stratagem or trick; here it refers to the cheetah's deliberate hiding as a hunting technique

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is not halal because the hiding breaks irsal
B) It is halal because the hiding is a hunting stratagem
C) It is halal only if the cheetah does not hide for long
D) It is makruh

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of game depends on the continuity of irsal; a pause for hunting strategy does not break irsal, but a pause for rest does.

Dog Catching Two Games After One Irsal
Ruling on dog catching two game sequentially after one irsal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a trained dog catches two games sequentially after a single irsal and explain the qiyas reasoning with the arrow analogy.

Core Idea
A single irsal can cover multiple catches if the dog's hunting action is uninterrupted.

In this lesson, we examine a ruling from the Hidayah concerning a trained hunting dog. The dog is sent after game, catches and kills one, then immediately catches another. The Hanafi school rules both are halal based on the continuity of the irsal. The passage draws an analogy with shooting an arrow that hits multiple targets, reinforcing that the act of sending remains effective until interrupted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What did the dog do? The hunter sent the dog (irsal) and the dog caught and killed one game, then immediately caught and killed another. The ruling: both are halal.
The ruling is clear from the text: both are eaten (akla jami'an).
2
Understand the key condition: irsal qaim
The ruling depends on the concept that the irsal (sending) is still standing (qaim) and not broken. This means the dog has not stopped, rested, or otherwise interrupted its hunting.
If the dog were to stop or return to the hunter, the irsal would break.
3
Recognise the qiyas (analogy)
The author compares to shooting an arrow that hits one game and then another. In that case, both are considered struck by the same shot. Similarly, the dog's continuous action is attributed to a single irsal.
The asl (original case) is the arrow, the far' (new case) is the dog.
4
Determine the illa (effective cause)
The common cause (illa) is the unity and continuity of the act of sending. In both cases, there is a single uninterrupted action that produces multiple hits.
This is a jaliy (clear) qiyas because the illa is apparent.
5
Apply the ruling to similar cases
If the dog continues hunting without a break (rest, drinking, eating from the first game), subsequent catches are halal. If interrupted, a new irsal is required.
This principle can be extended to trained falcons or other hunting animals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کلب معلم کو شکاری نے چھوڑا اس نے ایک شکار کو مارا پھر دوسرے شکار کو مارا تو دونوں حلال ہیں ولو اخذ بالكلب صيدا فقتله ثم اخذ أخر فقتله وقد ارسله صاحبه اكلا جميعا لان الارسال قائم لم ينقطع وهو بمنزلة مالورمى سهما الى صيد فاصابه و اصاب أخر
A trained dog was released by a hunter; it killed one game, then killed another game, so both are halal. And if a dog catches a game and kills it, then catches another and kills it, while its owner had sent it, both are eaten because the irsal is standing, not broken, and it is like the case of someone who shoots an arrow at a game, hits it, and hits another.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hidayah: when a trained dog is sent after game, and it catches and kills one animal, then proceeds to catch and kill a second animal before the irsal breaks, both catches are halal. The author compares this to shooting an arrow: if the arrow hits a target and then continues to hit another, both are considered struck by the same shot. Similarly, the dog's continuous hunting action is attributed to the single irsal, as long as the dog does not stop and rest or deviate from the hunt. The passage is incomplete in the window, but the ruling is clear: the first and second catch are both halal because the irsal remains active. The analogy with the arrow reinforces the principle that a single act of sending can yield multiple valid catches if the animal's pursuit is uninterrupted and within the scope of its training.

Key Terms
ارسال قائم (irsāl qāʾim): The sending is still in effect; the dog has not stopped or broken the connection to the hunter's command

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Neither is halal because the dog should have returned
D) It depends on the species of the game

Key Takeaway
When a trained dog catches multiple game in a single uninterrupted pursuit, the original irsal covers all catches.

Two Prey Killed Consecutively by One Dog: Both Halal
Hanafi ruling on dog killing two prey consecutively, with qiyas proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a dog killing two prey consecutively and explain the qiyas (analogy) with an arrow that justifies the ruling.

Core Idea
One continuous irsal and tasmiyah covers multiple prey killed in immediate succession.

In this lesson, we examine a specific hunting scenario: a trained dog kills two prey in a single chase. The Hanafi school rules both halal. The source provides a clear analogy to an arrow hitting two targets. We will unpack the conditions of irsal and tasmiyah and the analogy that supports this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
Read the passage: a dog kills two prey one after the other. The ruling is that both are halal.
The key is that the dog's action is one continuous act.
2
Understand key terms: irsal and tasmiyah
Irsal is the owner sending the dog with intent; tasmiyah is saying Bismillah at sending. Both are conditions for halal hunting.
One tasmiyah at the start suffices for the whole act.
3
Analyze the qiyas (analogy)
The author compares the dog to an arrow: if an arrow hits two prey consecutively, both are halal because the flight is one act. Similarly, the dog's chase is one continuous irsal.
The illa (effective cause) is the continuity of the original act without interruption.
4
Confirm the scope
This ruling applies only when the killings are immediate and part of the same hunting effort, not if the dog pauses or is recalled.
If the dog stops and is sent again, it becomes a new irsal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کتے کو اس کے مالک نے چھوڑا اس نے شکار مارا پھر دوسرا مارا تو دونوں شکار حلال ہیں اس لئے کہ مالک کا ارسال ختم نہیں ہوا اور یہ فعل واحد ہے جس میں ایک تسمیہ کافی ہے، جیسے اگر کسی نے شکار کی طرف تیر پھینکا اور تیر اس کو لگ کر دوسرے شکار کو بھی لگا تو دونوں شکار حلال ہیں ایسے ہی یہاں بھی حلال ہوں گے۔
The owner released the dog, it killed prey, then killed another: both prey are halal because the owner's sending (irsal) has not terminated, and it is one act in which one tasmiyah (mentioning Allah's name) suffices. Just as if someone shot an arrow at prey and the arrow struck it and then struck another prey, both are halal; similarly here they will be halal.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof by analogy (qiyas). The passage establishes that if a trained dog kills two prey in immediate succession, both are permissible to eat. The reason is that the owner's act of sending the dog (irsal) continues as a single act, and one recitation of tasmiyah at the time of sending covers the entire hunting episode. The proof is an analogy to shooting an arrow: if an arrow hits two prey consecutively, both are halal because the arrow's flight is one continuous action. The same principle applies to the dog.

Key Terms
ارسال (irsal): The act of sending the hunting animal by its owner with the intention of hunting.
تسمیہ (tasmiyah): Saying 'Bismillah' when sending the hunting animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is halal because the original irsal continues.
B) It is haram because the dog acted without permission.
C) It is halal only if the hunter said tasmiyah twice.
D) It is makruh because the dog may have rested.

Key Takeaway
A single, continuous hunting act with one irsal and tasmiyah renders all prey from that act halal.

Dog Rests on First Prey Then Kills Second
Ruling on continuity of irsal in hunting with dogs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a dog that rests on its first prey before killing a second, and explain why the kill is not halal due to the termination of irsal.

Core Idea
A dog's prolonged rest on its prey cuts off the irsal, making a subsequent kill not halal.

This lesson examines a specific case in hunting with trained dogs: what happens if the dog, after catching one prey, rests on it for a long time before chasing and killing another? The Hanafi school distinguishes between a quick pause (part of the hunt) and a rest (indicating termination of the original sending). Understanding this distinction clarifies the condition of continuous irsal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Read the source: the dog kills the first prey, then crouches on it for a long part of the day, then another prey passes and it kills it.
Focus on the duration: 'long part of the day' indicates rest.
2
State the ruling
The second prey is not halal (not eaten). The text says 'la yu'kal'.
This is a clear prohibition.
3
Locate the legal cause (illa)
The reason given: 'liinqita' al-irsal' (because the irsal is cut off). The dog's prolonged stay terminates the original sending.
Irsal is the act of sending the dog with tasmiyah.
4
Distinguish from the previous case
The text contrasts with a situation where the dog does not rest (e.g., kills immediately or pauses briefly for ambush). In that case, the second kill is halal.
The key distinction is intent and duration: rest vs. hunting strategy.
5
Apply the principle
If the dog rests so that the original irsal no longer applies, a new irsal (with fresh tasmiyah) would be required for the second kill to be permissible.
This ruling underscores the importance of continuity in the act of sending.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قتل الأول فجثم عليه طويلا من النهار ثم مربه صيد اخر فقتله لا يوكل الثاني لانقطاع الارسال يمكثه لم يكن ذلك حيلة منه للاخذ وانما كان استراحة بخلاف ماتقدم
And if it killed the first, then crouched upon it for a long part of the day, then another prey passed by and it killed it, the second will not be eaten because the irsal is cut off by its staying; that was not a trick for catching but was rest, unlike what preceded.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This is a ruling explaining a distinction. If the dog, after killing the first prey, sits on it for a long time (implying rest, not hunting strategy), the irsal is considered terminated. Therefore, if it then kills another prey, that second prey is not halal because the original sending has ended and a new irsal (with tasmiyah) is required. This contrasts with the previous case where the dog did not rest and killed consecutively. Here, the dog's prolonged stay indicates rest, not a hunting tactic.

Key Terms
جثم (jathama): The dog crouches or sits on the prey for a noticeable period.
ارسال (irsal): The act of sending the hunting animal with the recitation of tasmiyah.
حيلة (hilah): A trick or stratagem.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Not halal, because the dog's rest terminated the irsal.
D) Not halal, because the dog was not sent for the rabbit.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of a second kill by a hunting animal depends on whether the animal's pause indicates a break in the irsal; prolonged rest renders the subsequent kill impermissible.

Falcon's Kill Requires Certainty of Sending
Ruling on falcon's kill when irsal is uncertain

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when irsal is uncertain and explain why doubt leads to prohibition.

Core Idea
Doubt about irsal makes the prey haram — permissibility requires certainty of human sending.

In Islamic hunting law, a trained falcon's kill is not automatically halal. The key condition is irsal — that a person intentionally sent the falcon to hunt. If there is doubt, the animal remains haram. This ruling protects against consuming what may be carrion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a trained falcon killing prey. The ruling is that it is not halal (haram) to eat the prey.
2
Identify the critical condition
The condition for permissibility is irsal (human sending). Irsal must be certain.
Irsal means the human intentionally releases the animal for hunting.
3
Understand the effect of doubt
When doubt (shakk) occurs about irsal, the prey becomes impermissible. The default principle is that permissibility is not established without certainty.
The principle 'al-yaqin la yazuulu bi al-shakk' applies.
4
Apply the ruling to the scenario
In the given scenario, it is unknown whether the falcon was sent. Therefore, the prey is not halal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو ان بازيا معلما اخذ صيدا فقتله ولا يدرى ارسله انسان ام لا لايوكل لوقوع الشك في الارسال ولا تثبت الاباحة بدونه
And if a trained falcon caught prey and killed it, and it is not known whether a person sent it or not, it will not be eaten because doubt has occurred in the irsal, and permissibility is not established without it.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This is a straightforward ruling: the permissibility of eating game caught by a hunting animal depends on certainty that the animal was sent by a person. If there is doubt—such as the falcon being found with a kill but no evidence of who sent it or if it was sent at all—the prey is not halal. Irsal is a condition; without certainty, the default is prohibition.

Key Terms
ارسال (irsal): The sending of the hunting animal by a human with the intention of hunting.
شک (shakk): Doubt about whether irsal occurred.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Halal, because the falcon is trained.
