C) Not halal, because irsal is in doubt.
D) Not halal, unless the falcon was seen chasing the partridge.

Key Takeaway
When irsal is uncertain, the kill is not halal; certainty is required for permissibility.

Falcon's Ambush Pause: When a Stop Does Not Break the Hunt
Ruling on falcon ambush pause with qiyas to dog

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on a falcon's brief ambush pause and distinguish it from a rest pause using qiyas.

Core Idea
A brief ambush pause does not break irsal; the prey is halal.

In hunting with trained birds, a common question is whether a momentary stop by the falcon invalidates the hunt. This lesson clarifies that if the pause is tactical (ambush) rather than restful, the irsal (sending) remains intact and the kill is halal. We will explore the analogy to a dog's similar behavior.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
State the act (falcon lands briefly, then kills) and the ruling (halal).
The ruling depends on the nature of the pause.
2
Distinguish ambush from rest
Understand the key distinction: ambush (kamin) is a short tactical pause for hunting strategy; rest (istirahah) is a longer break that breaks irsal.
The text specifies 'short while for ambush' vs. 'long time for rest'.
3
Trace the proof (qiyas)
The ruling is derived by analogy to a dog that pauses briefly for ambush. Identify the asl (dog), far (falcon), and illa (pause for hunting strategy).
The illa is the intention and brevity of the pause.
4
Apply the illa to the new case
Since the falcon's pause is for ambush, not rest, the same ruling applies: halal.
This is a khafi (subtle) qiyas because the illa is purpose-based.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
Compare with schools that require no pause at all. The Hanafi position allows brief tactical stops.
The practical difference: Hanafis allow a short ambush; others may consider any pause invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو ارسل بازيه المعلم على صيد فوقع على شئ ثم اتبع الصيد فاخذه وقتله فانه يوكل ولهذا اذا لم يمكث زمانا طويلا للاستراحة وانما مكث ساعة للكمين لما بينا في الكلب
And if he sent his trained falcon upon prey, and it landed on something, then followed the prey, caught it, and killed it, then it is eaten. This is when it did not stay a long time for rest but stayed a short while for ambush, based on what we explained regarding the dog.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This ruling provides an exception to the previous case. If the falcon lands briefly on a perch (e.g., a tree) as a tactical ambush—not for rest—the irsal is not considered broken. The short pause is part of the hunting strategy, just as a dog might pause momentarily. Therefore, the subsequent kill is halal. The proof is qiyas to the dog, where a short pause for ambush does not terminate irsal.

Key Terms
کمین (kamin): Ambush; a short tactical pause to surprise prey.
ارسال (irsal): Sending the trained hunting animal after prey.
استراحة (istirahah): Rest; a prolonged stop that breaks irsal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
A falcon's brief pause for ambush does not break irsal; the kill is halal by qiyas to a dog's similar pause.

Dog Strangles Prey Without Wound: Not Halal; Ikhtilaf on Bone-Breaking
Ruling on dog killing by strangulation and intra-Hanafi dispute on bone-breaking

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a dog kills prey by strangulation or bone-breaking and explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding whether broken bones constitute a sufficient wound.

Core Idea
For dog-hunted prey to be halal, an external wound drawing blood is required; breaking a bone without external bleeding is insufficient per the majority Hanafi position.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about the condition of wounding (jarh) in hunting by dogs. While all Hanafis agree that strangulation without any wound renders prey impermissible, there is disagreement about whether breaking a bone—an internal wound—suffices. Understanding this ikhtilaf clarifies the precise requirements for halal meat from hunting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is a dog killing prey by strangulation. The ruling is that it is not halal because no wound is made.
Strangulation without any wound is unanimously impermissible.
2
State the condition for halal hunting
The condition is that the dog must make a wound (jarh) that causes blood to flow.
The Arabic 'جرح ينتهض سببا لا نهار الدم' emphasizes a wound that draws blood.
3
Note the extension to bone-breaking
The text states that the principle indicates that merely breaking a bone (without external blood) is also not halal.
This is a qiyas: bone-breaking resembles strangulation in not producing blood flow.
4
Present Abu Hanifa's exception
Abu Hanifa holds that breaking a limb is an internal wound (jarh batin) sufficient like an external wound, so the prey is halal.
His view is based on considering internal injury as a valid wound.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The majority (zahir al-riwayah) position is that bone-breaking without external bleeding does not suffice; this is the relied-upon view for fatwa.
The text explicitly marks this as 'على ظاهر الرواية'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وان خنقه الكلب ولم يجرحه لم يوكل لان الجرح شرط على ظاهر الرواية على ما ذكرناه وهذا يدلك على انه لا يحل بالكسر وعن ابي حنيفة انه اذا كسر عضو فقتله لاباس باكله لانه جراحة باطنة فهى كالجراحة الظاهرة وجه الاول ان المعتبر جرح ينتهض سببا لا نهار الدم ولا يحصل ذلك بالكسر فاشبه التخنيق
He (al-Quduri) said: And if the dog strangled it and did not wound it, it will not be eaten because wounding is a condition according to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah) as we mentioned. And this indicates that it is not permissible by breaking (bones). And from Abu Hanifa: if it broke a limb and killed it, there is no harm in eating it because it is an internal wound, so it is like an external wound. The first view's evidence is that the consideration is a wound that causes blood to flow, and that is not achieved by breaking, so it resembles strangulation.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute. The ruling (attributed to al-Quduri and the zahir al-riwayah) states that if a dog kills prey by strangulation (without making an external wound), the prey is not halal. This is because the condition for halal hunting is that the dog causes a wound (jarh) that draws blood. Similarly, if the dog merely breaks a bone (without external blood), the rule is the same: not halal. However, a narration from Imam Abu Hanifa states that if the dog breaks a limb and kills the prey, it is halal because the broken bone is an internal wound, analogous to an external wound. The dominant position (zahir al-riwayah) insists on an external bloody wound, as breaking a bone does not release blood and is akin to strangulation.

Key Terms
جرح (jarh): A wound that causes blood to flow; external wound.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The well-known and authoritative transmission of the Hanafi school from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani's six books.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A dog catches a rabbit and kills it by breaking its neck without any external blood. According to the zahir al-riwayah, what is the ruling?
A) Halal, because the dog killed it.
B) Halal, because the neck is broken (internal wound).
C) Not halal, because no external wound was made.
D) Not halal, because the dog was not sent.

Key Takeaway
For dog-hunted prey to be halal, an external wound (jarh) that draws blood is required; breaking a bone without external bleeding does not suffice per the majority Hanafi position.

Core Idea
A valid hunt requires the hunter's dog alone – any sharing invalidates the catch.

Hunting with dogs is a specialized area of fiqh with strict conditions. The trained dog of a Muslim can produce halal game only if certain prerequisites are met. This passage introduces a key scenario where those conditions are breached: when another dog — untrained, owned by a Magian, or one that omitted tasmiyah — joins the hunt. Even though the passage is cut off, the ruling is clear: the prey is not halal.

6 min
The Role of Homogeneity in Hunting Participation
Clarifying that if a second dog returns prey without wounding, consumption is makruh tahrimi due to partial participation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a second dog participates in hunting without wounding, and explain why homogeneity (mujānasa) determines legal participation (mushāraka).

Core Idea
If a second dog only returns the prey without wounding, consumption is makruh tahrimi due to partial participation.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in hunting where two dogs are involved. You will learn how the Hanafi school uses the concept of homogeneity of actions (mujānasa) to determine whether participation (mushāraka) taints the catch. The key distinction is between a second dog that physically returns the prey but does not wound, and a human who does the same.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
The case: a second dog returns the prey to the first dog without wounding it; the first dog wounds and kills it. The ruling is that eating it is makruh (disliked).
The prey is not completely halal nor completely haram.
2
Locate the ratio (illah) for the ruling
The reason is that there is participation (mushāraka) in the act of taking (akhdh) but not in the act of wounding (jarh). This partial participation creates a prohibition that is less than full haram.
The illah is the combination of shared capture and exclusive wounding.
3
Apply the concept of homogeneity (mujānasa)
The second dog's action is of the same genus as the first dog's action (both are canine). Hence, their actions are considered homogeneous, establishing legal participation.
Homogeneity is necessary for participation; without it, no participation occurs.
4
Contrast with the human (Magian) scenario
If a Magian (non-Muslim) returns the prey to the dog, the prey is halal because the human's action is not homogeneous with the dog's action, so no participation exists. The catch is attributed solely to the trained dog.
The distinction hinges on the genus of the actor.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the degree of karaha
Shams al-A'immah al-Halwani held it as makruh tahrimi (prohibited dislike, close to haram). Some scholars said makruh tanzihi (better to avoid, not sinful). The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is tahrimi.
This shows that even within the Hanafi school, there can be nuance in the precise level of dislike.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و لو رده عليه الكلب الثاني ولم يجرحه معه ومات بجرح الاول يكره اكله لوجود المشاركة في الاخذ و فقدها في الجرح وهذا بخلاف ما اذا رده المجوسى عليه بنفسه حيث لا يكره لان فعل المجوسى ليس من جنس فعل الكلب فلا تتحقق المشاركة وتتحقق بين فعلى الكلبين لوجود المجانسة ترجمہ اور اگر لوٹا دیا شکار کو پہلے کتے پر دوسرے کتے نے اور اس نے پہلے کتے کے ساتھ اس کو زخمی نہیں کیا اور شکار مر گیا پہلے کتے کے زخم لگانے سے تو اس کا کھانا مکروہ ہے پکڑنے میں شرکت کے پائے جانے کی وجہ سے اور زخمی کرنے میں مشارکت کے مفقود ہونے کی وجہ سے اور یہ اس صورت کے خلاف ہے جب کہ مجوسی نے اس کو خود لوٹایا ہو اس حیثیت سے کہ یہ مکروہ نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ مجوسی کا فعل کتے کے فعل کی جنس سے نہیں ہے تو مشارکت متحقق نہ ہوگی اور مشارکت متحقق ہو جاتی ہے دو کتوں کے فعل کے درمیان مجانست کے پائے جانے کی وجہ سے۔ تشریح وہ دوسرا کتا شریک ہوا ہے اگر اس نے پکڑنے میں اور زخمی کرنے میں شرکت نہ کی ہو بلکہ صرف یہ کام کیا ہو کہ شکار کو دبا کر پہلے کتے کی طرف پھیر دیا اور پہلے کتے نے اس کو زخمی کر کے مار ڈالا تو یہاں من وجہ مشارکت ہے اور من وجہ نہیں ہے یعنی پکڑنے میں مشارکت ہے اور زخمی کرنے میں مشارکت نہیں ہے تو اس کا کھانا حرام نہ ہوگا اور نہ بالکل جائز ہوگا بلکہ مکروہ تحریمی ہے۔ اور مجوسی نے شکار کو کلب معلم کی طرف پھیر دیا ہو تو شکار حلال ہوگا کیونکہ مجوسی کا فعل کتے کے فعل کی ہم جنس نہیں ہے لہذا مشارکت نہیں پائی گئی اور چونکہ دونوں کتوں کا فعل ہم جنس ہے تو وہاں مشارکت موجود ہے۔ شمس الائمہ حلوائی نے کراہت کو کراہت تحریمی پر محمول کیا ہے اگر چہ بعض نے تنزیہی کہا ہے۔
And if the second dog returns it upon the first and does not wound it with him, and it dies from the wound of the first, its eating is makruh due to the existence of participation in taking and its absence in wounding. This is contrary to the case when a Magian returns it upon him by himself, for it is not makruh because the action of a Magian is not of the genus of the dog's action, so participation does not materialize. But participation does materialize between the actions of two dogs due to the existence of homogeneity. Translation: And if the second dog returns the prey to the first dog and does not wound it together with the first, and the prey dies from the wound of the first, then its eating is makruh because of the share in capturing and the lack of share in wounding. This is contrary to the case when a Magian himself returns it to him, for it is not makruh because the action of a Magian is not of the genus of the dog's action, so participation is not realized, but participation is realized between the actions of two dogs due to the existence of homogeneity. Elaboration: That second dog that participated: if it did not participate in capturing and wounding, but only did the work of pushing the prey and turning it towards the first dog, and the first dog wounded and killed it, then here there is participation from one aspect and not from another – that is, participation in capturing exists and participation in wounding does not. So its eating will not be haram and not absolutely permissible, but rather makruh tahrimi. And if a Magian turned the prey towards the trained dog, then the prey will be halal, because the action of a Magian is not of the same genus as the dog's action, therefore participation is not found. And since the actions of two dogs are homogeneous, there participation exists. Shams al-A'immah al-Halwani considered the karaha as karaha tahrimiyya, although some called it tanzihiyya.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof addressing a specific scenario in hunting. The case: a second dog (which may be untrained or otherwise) returns the prey to the first dog but does not wound it. The prey dies solely from the first dog's wound. The ruling is that consumption is makruh (disliked). The reason is that the second dog participated in capturing (khadh) by turning the prey back, but did not participate in wounding (jarh). Since both dogs shared in the act of taking the prey, but only one caused the fatal wound, the prey is not completely halal nor completely haram; it falls into the category of makruh tahrimi according to Shams al-A'immah al-Halwani, though some scholars considered it makruh tanzihi. The passage contrasts this with a scenario where a Magian (i.e., a non-Muslim) personally returns the prey to the dog. In that case, the prey is not makruh but halal because the Magian's action is not of the same genus as the dog's action (i.e., he is not a hunting animal), so there is no participation (musharaka) that would taint the catch. The distinction hinges on the homogeneity (mujānasa) of the actions: between two dogs, their actions are homogeneous (both are canine acts), so participation is established; between a human and a dog, there is no homogeneity, so participation does not exist, and the catch remains attributable solely to the trained dog. This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on causality and the attribution of the catch to the proper legal agent.

Key Terms
مشارکت (mushāraka): Legal participation in the act of capturing the prey, which affects the ruling on its permissibility.
مجانست (mujānasa): Homogeneity or similarity in genus/type between two actions, used to determine whether participation (mushāraka) exists.
کراہت تحریمی (karāha taḥrīmiyya): Prohibited dislike, a level of prohibition close to haram but based on a zanni evidence; requiring avoidance but not as severe as haram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a second dog returns the prey to the first dog without wounding it, and the first dog kills it, what is the Hanafi ruling on eating the prey?
A) Halal because the first dog killed it.
B) Haram because two dogs participated.
C) Makruh tahrimi due to participation in capturing but not wounding.
D) Makruh tanzihi, permissible but disliked.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi hunting law, participation (mushāraka) is based on homogeneity of action (mujānasa), and partial participation without wounding renders the prey makruh tahrimi.

Ruling on a Second Dog's Spur and a Magian's Shout
Explaining that if a second dog merely encourages the first (no return), the prey is halal, and similarly if a Magian shouts at the dog.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a second dog spurring the first without returning the prey and on a Magian's shout, and explain the principle of subsidiary action (taba') that determines permissibility.

Core Idea
A subsidiary action does not change legal attribution; the prey remains halal when a second dog merely encourages or a Magian shouts.

This passage from al-Hidayah presents two scenarios involving hunting with dogs: one where a second dog spurs the first without returning the prey, and another where a Magian shouts at a Muslim's trained dog. In both cases, the prey is halal because the second actor's action is subsidiary to the original dispatch. Understanding the concept of taba' (subsidiary) is key to applying these rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
In the first scenario, a second dog runs after the first dog, causing the first to increase speed and catch the prey. The ruling: the prey is halal. In the second scenario, a Magian shouts at a Muslim's trained dog, and the dog responds by increasing pursuit. The ruling: the prey is halal.
Both scenarios yield the same ruling despite different actors.
2
Analyze the second actor's action
Determine whether the second actor directly participated in catching the prey. In the first scenario, the second dog did not touch the prey; it only affected the first dog. In the second scenario, the Magian only shouted; the dog's response was natural.
The key is that the second actor did not independently capture or wound the prey.
3
Apply the principle of subsidiary action (taba')
Hanafi law attributes an action to its primary originator. Here, the second actor's action is subsidiary (taba') because it is based on and enhances the first actor's action. Therefore, legal attribution remains with the first actor.
This principle is analogized to abrogation: a later act cannot override an earlier one unless it is stronger or equal; zur is less than irsal, so it does not change attribution.
4
Contrast with independent action
If the second dog had returned the prey to the first dog, its action would be independent, and both dogs would share attribution. Here, the second dog merely spurred, so attribution is exclusive to the first.
The distinction hinges on whether the second action is independent or subsidiary.
5
State the final ruling and its basis
Both prey are halal. The basis is that the second actor's action did not independently contribute to the capture; it was subsidiary to the original dispatch. This is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school, recorded in Zahir al-Riwayah.
The ruling is mufta bihi.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

نے اپنا معلم کتا شکار پر چھوڑا اور وہ شکار کے پیچھے دوڑ رہا ہے اتنے میں دوسرا جاہل کتا کلب معلم کے پیچھے دوڑ پڑا جس کی وجہ سے کلب معلم کی رفتار تیز ہو گئی اور اس نے شکار پکڑ لیا تو یہ شکار حلال ہے کیونکہ دوسرے کتے کے فعل نے پہلے کتے میں کرنٹ پیدا کیا ہے شکار میں اس کا کوئی اثر نہیں ہے تو دوسرے کتے کا فعل پہلے کتے کے فعل کے تابع شمار کیا جائے گا کیونکہ پہلے کتے کا فعل مقدم ہے اور حکم کی اضافت بما هو فوقه او مثله كما في نسخ الآيات والزجر دون الارسال لكونه بناء عليه ترجمہ امام محمد نے جامع صغیر میں فرمایا ہے اور جب مسلمان نے اپنا کتا چھوڑا پس کسی مجوسی نے اس کو زجر کیا پس وہ اس کا زجر مان لیا گیا تو اس کا شکار کھانے میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہے اور زجر سے مراد کتے پر چلا کر للکارنا ہے اور انزجار سے مراد طلب کی زیادتی کو ظاہر کرنا ہے اور اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ فعل ختم ہوتا ہے ایسے فعل سے جو اس سے بڑھ کر یا اس کے مثل ہو جیسا کہ آیات کے نسخ میں اور زجر ارسال سے کمتر ہے زجر کے بناء ہونے کی وجہ سے ارسال پر۔ تشریح مسلمان نے اپنا معلم کتا تسمیہ پڑھ کر شکار پر چھوڑا تو کسی مجوسی نے کتے کو للکارا اور کتا اس کے زجر کو مان کر جوش سے دوڑنے لگا تو وہ
And if the second dog did not return it upon the first, but it became fierce upon the first until the first became fierce upon the prey, then caught and killed it, there is no harm in eating it. Because the action of the second had effect on the dispatched dog, not on the prey, as it increased the pursuit, so it became a follower of its action because it is based upon it. Therefore, the taking is not attributed to the follower, contrary to the case when it returned it upon him, because it did not become a follower, so it is attributed to both. Elaboration: In the first scenario, both dogs shared in taking but not in wounding; in this scenario, they do not share in taking either; only the first dog caught the prey, so it is halal. The case: Zaid released his trained dog after the prey, and it is chasing; meanwhile a second ignorant dog runs after the trained dog, causing the trained dog to speed up and catch the prey. This prey is halal because the second dog's action affected the first dog, not the prey, so its action is considered subsidiary to the first dog's action. When the second dog had returned the prey, its action was not subsidiary, so attribution was to both. As for the Magian's zur: Imam Muhammad said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: When a Muslim releases his dog, then a Magian shouts at it, and it responds by increasing its pursuit, there is no harm in eating its prey. Zur means shouting to incite, and inzijar means showing increased pursuit. The reasoning: an act is abrogated by a stronger or similar act, as in the abrogation of verses, and zur is less than irsal because it is based on irsal. Elaboration: The Muslim released his trained dog with tasmiyah; a Magian shouted at it, and the dog responded by eagerly chasing; the prey is halal.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage consists of two connected rulings. The first ruling addresses a scenario where a second dog does NOT return the prey to the first dog but instead runs after the first dog, causing it to become more energetic and catch the prey. In this case, the catch is halal because the second dog's action only affected the first dog (increasing its drive), not the prey itself. The second dog's action is considered subsidiary (taba') to the first dog's action, so the catch is attributed solely to the first dog. This is contrasted with the earlier case where the second dog actually returned the prey, in which the action was not subsidiary and thus the catch was attributed to both. The second ruling is about a Muslim's trained dog being shouted at (zajr) by a Magian. According to Imam Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir, if a Magian shouts at the dog and the dog responds by increasing its pursuit, the prey is halal. The reason is that the Magian's zur is subordinate to the Muslim's original irsal (dispatch); the action of the Magian does not constitute independent participation but merely enhances what was already begun. The sharh analogizes this to abrogation of verses: a later act can only override an earlier one if it is stronger or equivalent, but zur is less than irsal, so it does not change the legal attribution. Thus, the prey remains attributable to the Muslim's dispatch.

Key Terms
زجر (zajr): Shouting or yelling at a hunting dog to incite it to chase more vigorously.
انزجار (inzijār): The dog's responsive increase in pursuit upon being shouted at.
تبع (taba'): Subsidiary or follower; an action that is legally attributed to the primary actor rather than the one performing it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Haram because two dogs were involved.
B) Makruh because the second dog helped.
C) Halal because the second dog's action is subsidiary to the first.
D) Not permissible until the second dog is identified.

Key Takeaway
The prey remains halal when a second dog or a Magian merely encourages the first dog, because their actions are subsidiary (taba') and do not independently contribute to the capture.

Ruling When a Second Dog Does Not Turn Back the Prey
Ruling on a non-participating second dog in hunting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a second dog joins a hunt without turning back the prey and apply the Hanafi principle of subordination (taba'iyyah) to determine halal status.

Core Idea
If a second dog only increases the first's eagerness, the game is halal—actions are attributed to the principal.

This lesson examines a nuanced hunting scenario: a second dog joins but does not turn the prey back. The Hanafi school applies the principle of subordination (taba'iyyah) to determine that the game remains halal. We will explore the reasoning and the qiyas that justifies this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
What is the exact scenario? A second dog joins the hunt but does not turn the prey back to the first dog. The ruling: the game is halal.
The second dog's action only affected the first dog's eagerness, not the prey directly.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The text uses qiyas: analogy to the case where the first dog alone catches the game. That case is halal by consensus.
The proof is aqli (rational) based on the illa of subordination.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The illa is that the second dog's action is subordinate (tabi') because it only increased the first dog's eagerness without causing the capture. The hunt is attributed to the primary agent.
Key phrase: 'فكان تبعا لفعله' (it became secondary to his action).
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some Hanafi scholars (e.g., Shams al-A'imma al-Halwai) considered it makruh tahrimi, but the majority and the relied-upon view is halal.
The intra-Hanafi disagreement is resolved by the majority position.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The text indicates the preferred view is halal, based on the principle that actions are attributed to the principal actor.
Tarjih authority: The commentary states this is the relied-upon position.

Source Text

Shams al-A'imma al-Halwai interpreted the prohibition as makruh tahrimi, though some said tanzihi. If the second dog does not share in catching, the game is halal. And if the second dog did not turn it back to the first, but he rushed upon the first until he became eager upon the game and caught and killed it, there is no harm in eating it, because the action of the second had effect on the sent dog, not on the game, because he increased in seeking due to it, so it became secondary to his action, because it is based upon it, so the taking is not attributed to the secondary, contrary to the case where he turned it back upon him, because he did not become secondary, so it is attributed to both.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling for a case where a second dog joins the hunt but does not turn the prey back to the first dog. The ruling is that the game is halal. The reasoning is based on the principle that actions are attributed to the primary agent. The second dog's action only made the first dog more eager; it did not directly cause the capture. Therefore, it is considered subordinate to the first dog's action, and the hunt is attributed entirely to the first dog. However, if the second dog had turned the prey back to the first, then it would be considered a partner, and the game would be attributed to both, requiring further conditions for halal status.

Key Terms
زجر (zajar): Calling out to the dog, shouting.
انزجار (inzijar): The dog's response to the call; in this context, increased eagerness.
ارسال (irsal): Sending the dog to hunt.
تابع (tabi'): Subordinate, secondary, subsidiary.
كراہت تحریمی (karahah tahrimi): A disliked act that is closer to forbidden; requires avoidance.

Key Takeaway
Game is halal if the second dog only increased the first dog's eagerness without directly causing the capture.

6 min
Ruling When a Majusi Calls Out to a Muslim's Dog
Ruling on a Majusi's call to a Muslim's hunting dog

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of naskh analogy to determine whether a subordinate action nullifies a primary action in hunting.

Core Idea
A subordinate action cannot nullify a primary action; only equal or stronger actions do.

A Muslim sends his trained dog with tasmiyah. Then a Majusi calls the dog, and the dog becomes more eager. Is the game still halal? The Hanafi school says yes, based on a qiyas with abrogation (naskh). This lesson unpacks that reasoning and distinguishes between two meanings of 'zajar' (calling).

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and the ruling
Identify the act (Muslim sends dog, Majusi calls, dog responds eagerly) and the ruling: the game is halal.
The ruling is from Imam Muhammad's al-Jami' al-Saghir.
2
Identify the primary and secondary actions
Primary action: irsal (sending the dog) initiated by the Muslim. Secondary action: zajar (calling the dog) done by the Majusi. Note that the dog's increased eagerness (inzijar) shows it responded to the call.
The secondary action is built upon the primary; the dog was already sent.
3
Understand the analogy with naskh
The text invokes the principle 'an action is nullified only by something equal or superior,' drawn from the rules of Quranic abrogation (naskh). This is a qiyas: just as a weaker verse cannot abrogate a stronger one, a subordinate action cannot nullify a primary one.
The dalil is aqli (rational analogy).
4
Apply the principle to the case
Since zajar is weaker than irsal (it is dependent on and subsequent to the sending), it cannot nullify the original irsal. Therefore, the game is halal.
The hukm is mubah (permissible).
5
Distinguish the two meanings of zajar
Zajar can mean (1) repelling the dog (driving it away), which would break the sequence; (2) inciting the dog (urging it on). The ruling here applies only to the second meaning, where the dog's eagerness increases. If the Majusi repels the dog, the game is not halal.
This nuance prevents misapplication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسلمان کے کلب معلم کو مجوسی للکار دے تو شکار حلال ہوگا یا نہیں
قال واذا ارسل المسلم كلبه فزجره مجوسی فانزجر بزجره فلا بأس بصيده والمراد بالزجر الإغراء بالصياح عليه وبالإنجرار اظهار زيادة الطلب ووجهه ان الفعل يرفع بما هو فوقه او مثله كما في نسخ الأيات والزجر دون الارسال لكونه بناء عليه
ترجمہ امام محمد نے جامع صغیر میں فرمایا ہے اور جب مسلمان نے اپنا کتا چھوڑا پس کسی مجوسی نے اس کو زجر کیا پس وہ اس کا زجر مان لیا گیا تو اس کا شکار کھانے میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہے اور زجر سے مراد کتے پر چلا کر للکارنا ہے اور انزجار سے مراد طلب کی زیادتی کو ظاہر کرنا ہے اور اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ فعل ختم ہوتا ہے ایسے فعل سے جو اس سے بڑھ کر یا اس کے مثل ہو جیسا کہ آیات کے نسخ میں اور زجر ارسال سے کمتر ہے اس لئے زجر کے بناء ہونے کی وجہ سے ارسال پر۔
تشریح مسلمان نے اپنا معلم کتا تسمیہ پڑھ کر شکار پر چھوڑا تو کسی مجوسی نے کتے کو للکارا اور کتا اس کے زجر کو مان کر مرید دوڑنے لگا تو وہ شکار حلال ہوگا کیونکہ مجوسی کے زجر کی وجہ سے ارسال مسلم ختم نہیں ہوگا کیونکہ کسی فعل کا ختم ہو جانا اپنے سے قومی یا برابر کے فعل سے ہوتا ہے اور فعل مجوسی کمتر ہے تو اس سے فعل مسلم ختم نہ ہوگا، اور فعل مجوسی کے کمتر ہونے کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ وہ زجر ہے اور فعل مسلم ارسال ہے اور ارسال اصل ہے اور زجر تابع ہے اور تابع متبوع سے کمتر ہوتا ہے جس طرح آیت کا نسخ اپنے سے برتر یا مثل سے ہوتا ہے کمتر سے نہیں ہو تا لہذا حکم مجمل کی وجہ سے منسوخ نہیں ہوتا وفصلناه في درس الحسامی
زجر کے دو معنی ہیں
۱- مجوسی کتے کو دھتکارے اور شکار کا پیچھا کرنے سے روکے اور انزجار یہ کہ وہ رک جائے اور شکار نہ مارے پھر جا کر شکار مارے تو یہ شکار حلال نہ ہوگا۔
۲- مجوسی چیخ کر سکتے کو شکار پر برانگیختہ کرے اور انزجار یہ کہ وہ زیادہ دوڑنے لگے یہاں یہی معنی مراد ہیں جیسے ہمارے یہاں اغراء کی صورت یہ ہے اے بیٹے ۔ پھر کیا کرے گا وغیرہ۔
And when a Muslim sends his dog and a Majusi calls it and it responds, there is no harm in the game. The meaning of zajar is inciting by shouting at it, and inzijar is manifesting increased seeking. The rationale is that an action is abrogated by something stronger or equal, as in abrogation of verses. And zajar is lower than irsal because it is based upon it. Commentary: A Muslim sent his trained dog with tasmiyah, a Majusi called it, and the dog responded. The game is halal because the Majusi's call does not nullify the Muslim's irsal, since an action is only nullified by a superior or equal action. The Majusi's zajar is inferior, so it cannot nullify. The reason for inferiority is that zajar is subordinate to irsal. Just as abrogation of verses occurs by a stronger or similar verse, not by a weaker one. Then it explains two meanings of zajar: (1) to repel and stop the dog; in that case the game is not halal. (2) to excite and urge the dog on; this is the intended meaning here.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage addresses the situation where a Muslim sends his trained dog to hunt, and then a Majusi (fire-worshipper) calls out to the dog, and the dog responds by increasing its effort. The ruling is that the game remains halal. The proof is based on a qiyas with abrogation (naskh): an action is only annulled by an action stronger or equal to it. Since the Majusi's call (zajar) is a subordinate action (based on the Muslim's sending), it is weaker and cannot nullify the original sending. The text also clarifies that there are two meanings of zajar: one is to repel the dog (which would make the game haram), but the intended meaning here is to encourage the dog to hunt more eagerly. Thus, the game is halal.

Key Terms
زجر (zajar): Calling out to the dog; in this context, inciting it.
انزجار (inzijar): Response; the dog's increased eagerness.
نسخ (naskh): Abrogation; used as an analogy for overriding actions.
مجوسی (majusi): Fire-worshipper; a non-Muslim whose slaughter is invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a Majusi calls out to a Muslim's hunting dog and the dog becomes more eager, is the game halal?
A) Yes, because the Majusi's call is subordinate
B) No, because the Majusi invalidates the hunt
C) Only if the dog does not respond
D) It is makruh

Key Takeaway
A subordinate action, like the Majusi's incitement, cannot nullify the primary action of a Muslim sending his dog.

Ruling When a Muslim Calls Out to a Majusi's Dog
Ruling with qiyas proof on Muslim calling a Majusi's dog

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a Muslim calls a dog sent by a Majusi and explain the reasoning based on qiyas.

Core Idea
A Muslim's call cannot override the invalid origin of the sending.

In the previous lesson, we learned that a Majusi's call on a Muslim's dog does not prohibit the game. Here we examine the converse: a Muslim calls a Majusi's dog. The ruling is that the game is not halal. The Hanafi position uses qiyas reasoning and extends this to all persons whose slaughter is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a Muslim calling a dog that was sent by a Majusi. The ruling is that the game caught is not halal (haram).
This is the central ruling of the passage.
2
Understand the qiyas reasoning
The text uses qiyas: zajar (call) is inferior to irsal (sending). Since a Majusi's call on a Muslim's dog could not establish prohibition, a Muslim's call on a Majusi's dog cannot establish permissibility.
The argument is a fortiori (first awla).
3
Extend to other invalid hunters
The passage extends the ruling to anyone whose slaughter is invalid: apostate (murtad), person in ihram (muhrim), and one who deliberately omits tasmiyah. In all these cases, if they send the dog and a Muslim calls it, the game is not halal.
These categories are in the rank of Majusi in this context.
4
Confirm the authority
The ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah and is agreed upon (muttafaq alayh) with no intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
Mufta bihi: not halal (haram).
5
Apply to a new scenario
Consider a dog sent by an apostate. A Muslim calls it and it catches game. According to this lesson, the game is not halal because the sender's status invalidates the hunt.
The caller's status does not rectify the invalid sending.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مجوسی کے کتے کو مسلمان للکار دے تو شکار کا حکم
ولو ارسله مجوسی فزجره مسلم فانزجر لم يوكل لان الزجردون الارسال ولهذا لم تثبت به شبهة الحرمة فاولى ان لا يثبت به الحل وكل من لا تجوز ذكاته كالمرتد والمحرم و تارک التسمية عامدا في هذا
بمنزلة المجوسى
ترجمہ اور اگر کتے کو مجوسی نے چھوڑا ہو پس مسلمان نے اس کو زجر کیا پس وہ زجر مان گیا تو کھایا نہیں جائے گا اس لئے کہ زجر ارسال سے کمتر ہے اسی وجہ سے حرمت کا شبہ ثابت نہیں ہوا تھا پس بدرجہ اولی اس حلت ثابت نہ ہوگی اور مردہ شخص جس کی زکاۃ جائز نہیں ہے جیسے مرتد ومحرم اور تسمیہ چھوڑنے والا اسی سلسلہ میں مجوسی کے درجہ میں ہے۔
تشریح جیسے پہلی صورت میں مجوسی کے زجر سے حرمت یا حرمت کا شبہ ثابت نہیں ہوا اسی طرح صورت مذکورہ میں مسلمان کے زجر سے بدرجہ اولی حلت ثابت نہ ہو گی۔ پھر مجوسی نے ارسال کیا ہو یا محرم نے یا مرتد نے یا عمد التسمیہ چھوڑنے والے نے اور پھر مسلمان نے زجر کیا ہو تو حلت ثابت نہ ہوگی اور جن کی زکوۃ جائز نہیں ہے وہ اس باب میں مجوسی کے درجہ میں ہیں۔
And if a Majusi sent it and a Muslim called it and it responded, it is not eaten because zajar is less than irsal, and for this reason a semblance of prohibition was not established, so it is even less likely to establish permissibility. And everyone whose slaughter is not permissible, like an apostate, a muhrim (state of ihram), and one who deliberately omits tasmiyah, are in the same rank as the Majusi in this regard. Commentary: Just as in the previous case the Majusi's call did not establish prohibition or its semblance, here the Muslim's call does not establish permissibility. So if a Majusi, or a muhrim, or an apostate, or one who deliberately omits tasmiyah sent the dog, and then a Muslim called it, the game is not halal. Those whose slaughter is not permissible are like the Majusi in this chapter.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage deals with the reverse scenario: a Majusi sends his dog, and a Muslim calls it. The ruling is that the game is not halal. The reasoning is that if the Majusi's call could not make the hunt prohibited (in the case of a Muslim's dog), then a Muslim's call should not make it permissible when the dog is from a Majusi, because the call (zajar) is inferior to the sending (irsal). The text also extends this to other individuals whose slaughter is invalid: apostates, those in ihram, and those who deliberately omit tasmiyah. In all these cases, the game is not halal.

Key Terms
مرتد (murtad): An apostate from Islam; his slaughter is invalid.
محرم (muhrim): A person in the state of ihram (for Hajj/Umrah); cannot hunt.
تارک التسمية عامدا (tarik al-tasmiyah 'amdan): One who deliberately omits saying Bismillah before hunting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a Majusi sends his dog and a Muslim calls it, is the game halal?
A) Yes, because the Muslim called it
B) No, because the Majusi's sending is invalid
C) Only if the dog responds
D) It is makruh

Key Takeaway
The validity of hunting depends on the one who sends the dog, not on the one who calls it.

Ruling When a Muslim Calls a Stray Dog and Hunts
Ruling on a Muslim calling a stray dog and hunting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hunting with a stray dog called by a Muslim and explain the reasoning equating the call to sending.

Core Idea
A Muslim's call to a stray dog with tasmiyah is legally equivalent to sending the dog, making the hunt halal.

In this lesson, we examine a specific scenario: a stray dog not sent by any owner. The Hanafi school rules that if a Muslim calls the dog with tasmiyah and it hunts, the game is halal. The reasoning hinges on a legal analogy between the call (zajr) and the dog's self-release (infitad), with the crucial factor being the action of a responsible person (mukallaf).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is hunting with a stray dog that is called by a Muslim with tasmiyah. The ruling is that the game is halal (mubah).
2
Understand the condition
The dog must not have been sent by any owner; it is stray. The Muslim must call it (zajr) and say tasmiyah. The dog must respond and catch the game.
The call is a deliberate action, not just any noise.
3
Analyze the proof (dalil)
The proof is a rational analogy: the call (zajr) is like self-release (infitad) because it causes the dog to hunt, but it is superior because it is an act of a responsible person (mukallaf). Thus it overrides the lack of ownership.
The term 'nasikh' here means 'overriding' not Quranic abrogation.
4
Note the cross-madhhab status
This ruling is muttafaq alayh (agreed upon) among the schools; no dispute recorded in the source.
5
Apply the ruling
Any scenario meeting the conditions (stray dog, call, tasmiyah, response) yields halal game. Missing any condition makes it not halal.
For example, if the Muslim only says tasmiyah without calling, the game is not halal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غير مرسل کتے کو مسلمان نے للکارا اور تسمیہ پڑھی تو شکار حلال ہے
وان لم يرسله احد فزجره مسلم فانزجر فاخذ الصيد فلا بأس باكله لان الزجر مثل الانفلات لانه إن كان دونه من حيث انه بناء عليه فهو فوقه من حيث انه فعل المكلف فاستويا فصلح بالناسخ
ترجمہ اور اگر کتے کو کسی نے نہیں چھوڑا پس مسلمان نے اس کو زجر کیا (اور تسمیہ پڑھا) پس وہ زجر مان گیا پس اس نے شکار پکڑا تو اس کے کھانے میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ زجر خود چلنے کے مثل ہے (اور برابر سے نسخ ہو سکتا ہے) اس لئے کہ زجر اگر چہ خود چھوٹے سے کم ہے اس حیثیت سے کہ زجر خود چھوٹے پر مبنی ہے پس زجر خود چھوٹنے سے بڑھ کر ہے ایسی حیثیت سے کہ وہ مکلف کا فعل ہے تو دونوں برابر ہو گئے پس یہ (زجر) ناسخ بننے کی صلاحیت رکھے گا۔
And if no one sent it, and a Muslim called it and it responded and caught the game, there is no harm in eating it, because zajar is like self-release (infitad). Because although it is less than it in that it is based upon it, it is above it in that it is the action of a morally responsible person, so they become equal, and thus it is suitable for abrogation. Translation: And if the dog was not sent by anyone, then a Muslim called it (and said tasmiyah), and it responded and caught the game, there is no harm in eating it, because zajar (calling) is like self-release (infitad). Because although it is less than sending from the aspect that it is based on the dog's own action, it is greater from the aspect that it is the action of a responsible person, so they become equal, and so this (zajar) is fit to be a source (naskh).
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This final case covers a dog that is not sent by any owner but is stray. If a Muslim calls it with tasmiyah and it hunts, the game is halal. The reasoning is that the Muslim's call (zajar) is considered equivalent to sending the dog, because it is an intentional act of a responsible person. Although the dog is not owned, the call is a deliberate action that initiates the hunt, similar to releasing the dog. Therefore, the game is halal.

Key Terms
انفلات (infitad): Self-release; the dog going on its own without being sent.
مکلف (mukallaf): A morally responsible person; one who is accountable for actions.
ناسخ (nasikh): That which abrogates; here, an action that can override another.
زجر (zajr): Calling/urging the dog; an intentional shout or command.
تسمیہ (tasmiyah): Saying 'Bismillah' (in the name of Allah) before the hunt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because the call is like sending
B) No, because the dog was not sent
C) Only if the dog is trained
D) It is makruh

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school equates a Muslim's intentional call to a stray dog with sending it, making the hunt halal.

The Ineffectiveness of a Muslim's Shout on a Magian's Hunting Dog
Ruling with proof using a fortiori analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on game killed by a dog sent by a Magian when a Muslim shouts, and explain the a fortiori (qiyas aulawi) analogy used as proof.

Core Idea
The inferior cannot override the superior: a Muslim's shout cannot make lawful what the Magian's sending made suspect.

In this passage from Al-Hidayah, we examine a specific hunting scenario: a Magian sends his dog to hunt, but a Muslim shouts at the dog, causing it to desist. Does this Muslim intervention make the game lawful? The Hanafi school rules it does not, basing the ruling on a clear rational analogy. We will unpack the legal reasoning, the categories of prohibited hunters, and the underlying principle that the inferior action (zajr) cannot overcome the superior action (irsal).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Parties
First, note that the sending (irsal) of the dog was done by a Magian (majusi), whose slaughter is invalid. The shout (zajr) was done by a Muslim. Determine whose action is primary: the sending.
The act here is the sending of the dog, not the shouting.
2
Determine the Hukm (Ruling)
The text says 'lam yukal' (it is not eaten). Identify the hukm level: it is haram (prohibited). The game is unlawful.
The ruling is absolute: even though a Muslim shouted, the game remains unlawful.
3
Locate the Dalil (Proof)
The proof is given in the phrase 'lianna al-zajr duna al-irsal wa li hadha lam tathbut bihi shubhat al-hurmati, fa-awla an la yathbuta bihi al-hillu'. Recognize this as a qiyas aulawi (a fortiori analogy).
Qiyas aulawi is an 'even more so' argument.
4
Analyze the Analogy (Qiyas Aulawi)
The analogy works in two steps: (a) In an earlier case (presumably where a Magian shouts at his own dog), the shout did not even create a suspicion (shubha) of prohibition. (b) Therefore, a fortiori, a Muslim's shout cannot establish permissibility (hill) when the sending was by a Magian.
The key point: if a lesser effect (shubhat al-hurmi) is not produced by the Majian's shout, then a greater effect (definite hill) is even less likely from a Muslim's shout.
5
Extend the Ruling to Similar Cases
The text analogizes all persons whose slaughter is invalid (apostate, muhrim, one who intentionally omits tasmiyah) to the Magian. Understand that for all such persons, the same ruling applies: the Muslim's shout cannot make the game lawful.
This extends the ruling beyond Magians to any hunter whose slaughter is not valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مجوسی کے کتے کو مسلمان للکار دے تو شکار کا حکم
ولو ارسله مجوسی فزجره مسلم فانزجر لم يوكل لان الزجردون الارسال ولهذا لم تثبت به شبهة الحرمة فاولى ان لا يثبت به الحل وكل من لا تجوز ذكاته كالمرتد والمحرم و تارک التسمية عامدا في هذا بمنزلة المجوسى
ترجمہ اور اگر کتے کو مجوسی نے چھوڑا ہو پس مسلمان نے اس کو زجر کیا پس وہ زجر مان گیا تو کھایا نہیں جائے گا اس لئے کہ زجر ارسال سے کمتر ہے اسی وجہ سے حرمت کا شبہ ثابت نہیں ہوا تھا پس بدرجہ اولی اس حلت ثابت نہ ہوگی اور مردہ شخص جس کی زکاۃ جائز نہیں ہے جیسے مرتد ومحرم اور تسمیہ چھوڑنے والا اسی سلسلہ میں مجوسی کے درجہ میں ہے۔
تشریح جیسے پہلی صورت میں مجوسی کے زجر سے حرمت یا حرمت کا شبہ ثابت نہیں ہوا اسی طرح صورت مذکورہ میں مسلمان کے زجر سے بدرجہ اولی حلت ثابت نہ ہو گی۔ پھر مجوسی نے ارسال کیا ہو یا محرم نے یا مرتد نے یا عمد التسمیہ چھوڑنے والے نے اور پھر مسلمان نے زجر کیا ، ہو تو حلت ثابت نہ ہوگی اور جن کی زکوۃ جائز نہیں ہے وہ اس باب میں مجوسی کے درجہ میں ہیں۔
Ruling on hunting when a Muslim shouts at a Magian's dog: And if a Magian sends (a dog) and a Muslim shouts at it, and it desists, it is not eaten because the shout is inferior to sending. For this reason, a suspicion of prohibition was not established, so it is more appropriate that permissibility is not established. And everyone whose slaughter is not permissible, like an apostate, a muhrim, and one who intentionally omits the tasmiyah, are in this regard like a Magian. Translation: And if a Magian sent a dog, then a Muslim shouted at it, and it desisted, then it will not be eaten because the shout is inferior to sending; for this reason, a suspicion of prohibition was not established, so a fortiori permissibility will not be established. And the person whose slaughtered meat is not permissible, such as an apostate, a muhrim, and one who intentionally omits the tasmiyah, are in this category like a Magian. Commentary: Just as in the first scenario, the Magian's shout did not establish prohibition or suspicion of prohibition, similarly in the mentioned scenario, the Muslim's shout will a fortiori not establish permissibility. Then whether a Magian sent (the dog) or a muhrim sent, or an apostate sent, or one who intentionally omitted the tasmiyah sent, and then a Muslim shouted, permissibility will not be established. And those whose slaughter is not permissible are in this chapter like a Magian.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning hunting when a Muslim shouts at a dog that was sent by a Magian or other prohibited person. The ruling is that the game is not lawful. The proof is a rational analogy: the shout (zajr) is inferior to the original sending (irsal). Since in a prior case a Magian's shout did not even create a suspicion of prohibition, a Muslim's shout, being even less effective, cannot establish permissibility. The text extends the ruling to all persons whose slaughter is invalid: apostates, those in ihram, and those who intentionally omit the tasmiyah. They are treated like Magians in this regard.

Key Terms
زجر (zajr): A shout or rebuke to turn the dog away from the game.
ارسال (irsal): Sending the dog to hunt.
ذكاته (dhakatuhu): The slaughter of the animal in a permissible manner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for the ruling that the game is not eaten?
A) Because the Muslim did not recite tasmiyah before shouting.
B) Because the shout is inferior to the sending, and permissibility cannot be established a fortiori.
C) Because the Magian did not own the dog.
D) Because the dog was not trained.

Key Takeaway
A Muslim's shout cannot make lawful the game of a dog sent by a person whose slaughter is invalid, because the shout is inferior to the original sending.

Ruling on Hunting When a Muslim Shouts at an Unleashed Dog
Ruling on permissibility of game when a Muslim shouts at an unleashed dog

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a Muslim shouts at a dog not sent by anyone, and explain the proof based on equality and abrogation.

Core Idea
A Muslim's shout equals the dog's own pursuit, making the game lawful as if he sent it.

This lesson addresses a nuanced scenario in hunting: a dog chases game on its own without being sent, and a Muslim shouts at it with tasmiyah. The Hanafi school permits eating the catch, applying a unique legal reasoning that the shout is equivalent to the dog's own pursuit, allowing abrogation of the dog's action. We will examine the ruling, its proof, and essential conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the act in question: a Muslim shouts at a dog that was not sent. The ruling is that the game is lawful (mubah) to eat.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is a rational analogy (qiyas) based on the principle that abrogation (naskh) is valid between equals.
The source states: 'lianna al-zajra mithla al-infial' (the shout is like the dog's own pursuit).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Understand why they are considered equal: although the dog's pursuit (infi'al) is the original action, the shout (zajr) is an action of a responsible person (mukallaf). Thus, each has a superior aspect, balancing them.
The text balances two perspectives: infi'al is superior as the original, but zajr is superior as a human act.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Other schools may not accept naskh by an equal and may require the dog to be originally sent. This is a legitimate difference.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi within the school.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi school relies on this reasoning as part of Zahir al-Riwayah. The mufta bihi is the permissibility under the stated conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غیر مرسل کتے کو مسلمان نے للکارا اور تسمیہ پڑھی تو شکار حلال ہے
وان لم يرسله احد فزجره مسلم فانزجر فاخذ الصيد فلا بأس باكله لان الزجر مثل الانفلات لانه إن كان دونــه مــن حيث انه بناء عليه فهو فوقه من حيث انه فعل المكلف فاستويا فصلح بـاســخــا
ترجمہ اور اگر کتے کو کسی نے نہیں چھوڑا پس مسلمان نے اس کو جر گیا ( اور تسمیہ پڑھا) پس وہ زجر مان گیا پس اس نے شکار پکڑا تو اس کے کھانے میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہے اس لئے کہ زجر خود چلنے کے مثل ہے ( اور برابر سے نسخ ہو سکتا ہے ) اس لئے کہ زجر اگر چہ خود چھوٹے سے کم ہے اس حیثیت سے کہ زجر خود چھوٹے پر مبنی ہے پس زجر خود چھوٹنے سے بڑھ کر ہے ایسی حیثیت سے کہ وہ مکلف کا فعل ہے تو دونوں برابر ہو گئے پس یہ ( زجر ) ناسخ بننے کی صلاحیت رکھے گا۔

Ruling on hunting when a Muslim shouts at an unleashed dog: And if no one sends it, and a Muslim shouts at it, and it desists, and then takes the game, there is no harm in eating it, because the shout is like the dog's own launching. For although it is inferior to it in that it is based on it, it is superior in that it is an action of a responsible person, so they become equal, and thus it is valid by abrogation. Translation: And if no one sent the dog, then a Muslim shouted at it (and recited tasmiyah), and it desisted, and then it caught the game, then there is no problem in eating it, because the shout is like the dog's own pursuit (and abrogation is valid from an equal). Because although the shout is inferior to the dog's own pursuit in that it is based on it, it is superior in that it is the action of a responsible person, so they become equal, and thus this (shout) becomes capable of abrogation. Commentary: If no one sent the dog, but it chased the game on its own, and it is a trained dog, and a Muslim shouted at it and recited tasmiyah, then its game is lawful. Because the established principle is that abrogation is valid by an equal. Here, the dog's own pursuit and the Muslim's shout are equal, so the dog's own pursuit is abrogated by the Muslim's shout, and it is now considered as if the Muslim sent the dog. The equality is because in one respect, the dog's launching is superior as it is the original and followed, and in another respect, the shout is superior as it is the action of a responsible person, so they are brought to the same level.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling and reasoning for a scenario where a Muslim shouts at a dog that was not sent by anyone but is chasing game on its own. The ruling is that the game is lawful if the Muslim recites tasmiyah. The proof is a rational analogy: the dog's own pursuit (infi'al) and the Muslim's shout (zajr) are considered equal in legal effect. This is because although the dog's pursuit is the original action, the shout is an action of a morally responsible person. Since abrogation (naskh) is valid among equals, the shout abrogates the dog's independent action, and the dog is now considered sent by the Muslim. Key conditions: the dog must be trained (mu'allam) and the Muslim must recite tasmiyah.

Key Terms
انفلات (infi'al): The dog's own pursuit of the game without being sent by a human.
نسخ (naskh): Abrogation, i.e., the Muslim's shout overrides and replaces the dog's own action.

B) The dog must have been originally sent by the Muslim.
C) The dog must be trained and the Muslim must recite tasmiyah.
D) The Muslim must shout at least twice.

Key Takeaway
A trained dog's own pursuit is abrogated by a Muslim's shout with tasmiyah, making the catch lawful as if the Muslim sent the dog.

Cooperative Hunting by Two Dogs: Permissibility of Game
Ruling on permissibility when two dogs from two owners cooperate in hunting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on game killed by the combined action of two dogs from two Muslim owners, and explain the underlying reasoning (ta'lil) that renders it permissible.

Core Idea
When two dogs from separate owners cooperate in hunting, the game is lawful because the second dog's fatal strike is excused beyond its training.

This lesson extends the previous ruling on a single dog's action to a scenario where two dogs from two Muslim owners act together. One dog stuns the game, the other kills it. The Hanafi school rules the game lawful, relying on the same reasoning: the second dog's action is not a violation of proper hunting procedure but is excused. Understanding this extension clarifies that permissibility does not depend on a single dog performing all acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Two Muslim men each send their own trained dog. One dog stuns the game (wounding but not killing), the other dog kills it. Determine the legal status of the game.
The dogs are from different owners, and the killing is performed by the second dog.
2
Note the ruling
The text states the game is lawful (halal) and can be eaten. Identify the ruling as 'mubah' (permissible) based on the matn.
The key phrase is 'akala' (it is eaten).
3
Trace the reasoning (ta'lil)
The text refers back to the reason given in the previous lesson: the second dog's strike is beyond its training and therefore excused. Explain why this reasoning applies here.
Training involves catching and killing; if the first dog stuns, the second dog's kill is not a failure of training but an independent act.
4
Address potential ownership question
The commentary raises a question about ownership of the game. Note that the passage does not resolve this, but the permissibility ruling stands regardless of ownership allocation.
Ownership is a separate issue; the lesson focuses on the permissibility of consumption.
5
Compare with single-dog case
Contrast this scenario with the case where one dog both stuns and kills. The reasoning is consistent: the killing action is excused in both scenarios because it is not a breach of training.
The extension shows the principle is not owner-specific.

The reason is the same as in the previous case: the second dog's action is excused because it is beyond training to expect it to refrain after wounding. The Muslim's Call as a Substitute for Sending the Hunting Dog
Explains that a Muslim's call abrogates the dog's own chase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a Muslim's call (zajr) with tasmiyah renders game halal when a trained dog chases on its own, using the Hanafi principle of naskh between equal causes.

Core Idea
A Muslim's call can replace the act of sending a hunting dog because an equal cause can abrogate another equal cause.

This passage addresses an edge case in hunting law: what if a trained dog pursues game without being sent by its owner? The Hanafi school rules that if a Muslim calls it back with tasmiyah, the catch is halal. The reasoning hinges on a fascinating application of the usul principle of naskh (abrogation) between equal causes. Let's unpack the logic and see how the dog's own pursuit and the Muslim's call are balanced.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
The scenario is that a trained dog (kalb mu'allam) chases game on its own (infilat) without being sent by anyone. A Muslim sees this, calls the dog back (zajr), and recites the tasmiyah (Bismillah).
Note that the dog is already trained; this is a condition for the ruling.
2
Recognize the Ruling
The ruling is that the game caught under these circumstances is halal (mubah). This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.
The ruling is not automatic; it requires the call and tasmiyah by a Muslim.
3
Locate the Proof
The proof is a rational principle (dalil aqli): the rule that abrogation (naskh) is valid between equal causes. This principle is applied here to equate the dog's own pursuit and the Muslim's call.
The principle is taken from usul al-fiqh, applied here through istihsan.
4
Analyze the Equality
The author explains why infilat and zajr are considered equal: infilat is original and independent (as the dog initiated the chase), but zajr is the act of a legally responsible person (mukallaf). Each has a superior aspect, so they balance out.
This balancing process is a form of taarud (conflict) resolution.
5
Apply the Principle
Since they are equal, the call abrogates the dog's independent pursuit. Consequently, the dog is treated as if it were sent by the Muslim, fulfilling the requirement of a deliberate sending for lawful hunting.
The practical effect: the game is halal, just as if the owner had sent the dog.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تشریح اگر کتے کو کسی نے نہیں چھوڑا بلکہ وہ خود شکار کے پیچھے دوڑا اور وہ کلب معلم ہے اور کسی مسلمان نے اس کو زجر کیا اور تسمیہ پڑھا تو اس کا شکار حلال ہے۔ کیونکہ اصول یہ مقرر ہے کہ اپنے برابر والے سے نسخ جائز ہے تو یہاں خود شکار کا تعاقب کرنا اور مسلمان کا زجر کرنا برابر ہے تو زجر مسلم سے خود کتے کا شکار کا تعاقب منسوخ ہو جائے گا اور اب یوں سمجھیں گے کہ مسلمان نے اس کو چھوڑا ہے اور برا بر ہونے کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ ایک حیثیت سے انفلات اعلیٰ ہے اور ایک حیثیت سے زجر اعلیٰ ہے۔ انفلات اس لئے اعلیٰ ہے کہ وہ اصل ہے اور متبوع ہے اور زجر مسلم تابع ہے اور اول پر مبنی ہے۔ اور زجر اس لئے اعلیٰ ہے کہ یہ مکلف کا فعل ہے اور انفلات غیر مکلف کا اس اعتبار سے زجر اعلیٰ ہو گیا۔ تو ان دونوں کو برابر کے درجہ میں اتار لیا گیا اور برابر سے نسخ جائز ہے تو انفلات منسوخ ہو گیا اور فقط زجر باقی رہ گیا تو یہ ایسا ہو گیا گویا مسلمان نے اس کتے کو شکار پر چھوڑا ہے۔
Explanation: If no one sent the dog, but it itself ran after the game, and it is a trained dog, and a Muslim called it back (zajr) and recited the tasmiyah, then its game is halal. Because the principle is established that abrogation from an equal is permissible. Here, the dog's own pursuit and the Muslim's calling are equal. So the dog's own pursuit is abrogated by the Muslim's calling, and it will be considered as if the Muslim sent it. The reason for equality is that from one aspect, infilat (dog's own pursuit) is superior, and from one aspect, zajr (Muslim's call) is superior. Infilat is superior because it is the original and independent, and the Muslim's calling is dependent and based on the first. Zajr is superior because it is the act of a legally responsible person, while infilat is the act of one not legally responsible. So they are brought to the same level, and abrogation from an equal is permissible, so infilat is abrogated and only zajr remains. So it becomes as if the Muslim sent that dog on the game.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. The author discusses a scenario where a trained dog chases game on its own (infilat) without being sent by anyone. If a Muslim calls the dog back (zajr) and utters the tasmiyah, the game becomes halal. The reasoning is based on a legal principle derived from analogy: abrogation (naskh) is valid between equal things. Here, the dog's independent pursuit and the Muslim's call are considered equal after balancing their strengths and weaknesses. The dog's action is original and independent, but the Muslim's action is the act of a morally accountable person. By equalizing them, the dog's pursuit is effectively canceled, and the dog is treated as if it were sent by the Muslim. This ensures that the conditions for lawful hunting (sending by a Muslim, tasmiyah, trained dog) are met.

Key Terms
زجر (zajr): Action of calling back or restraining a hunting dog by a Muslim.
انفلات (infilat): Dog chasing game on its own volition without being sent.
نسخ (naskh): Abrogation; here used in the sense of one legal cause canceling another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The dog's training alone.
B) The Muslim's call with tasmiyah.
C) The dog catching the game immediately.
D) The hunter's intention to hunt.

Key Takeaway
A Muslim's call (zajr) with tasmiyah can substitute for the act of sending a trained hunting dog, making the game halal through the principle of naskh between equal causes.

Ruling on Dog Immobilizing Then Killing Game
Ruling with rational proof on dog immobilizing then killing game

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a trained dog immobilizes then kills game, and explain the rational proof (dalil aqli) that justifies it.

Core Idea
A dog's kill after immobilization is excused because refraining from killing is not trainable.

In Hanafi fiqh, hunting with trained dogs is permitted under conditions. A unique question arises when the dog, after catching the game, first immobilizes it (wakahadh) and then kills it. Is the game halal or must the owner perform dhabihah? This lesson presents the Hanafi ruling and its elegant rational proof based on the limits of dog training.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Note the three elements: a Muslim hunter, a trained dog, and the sequence of actions: dog catches, immobilizes, then kills the game (or two dogs: one immobilizes, the other kills).
The tasmiyah must be recited when sending the dog.
2
State the ruling
The game is halal (permissible to eat) according to the Hanafi school.
This is the explicit conclusion in the text: 'akl' (eaten).
3
Examine the proof
The proof is rational: it is impossible to train a dog to stop after wounding and wait for the owner to slaughter. This inability means the dog's action is excused ('afw).
The Arabic phrase: 'la yadkhul taht al-ta'lim' (does not enter under training).
4
Understand the concept of 'afw
The term 'afw (excusable) is used for what cannot be controlled due to natural limitations, similar to forgetfulness in salah.
This is a unique Hanafi tool to avoid hardship.
5
Apply to variations
The same ruling applies when two dogs are involved: one immobilizes, the other kills.
Consistency of the principle: the kill after immobilization is always excused.

ولو ارسل المسلم كلبه على صید و سمی فادرکه فضربه ووقذه ثم ضربه فقتله اكل و كذا اذا ارسل كلبين فوقذه احدهما ثم قتله الاخر اكل لان الامتناع عن الجرح بعد الجرح لا يدخل تحت التعليم فجعل عفوا ترجمہ اور اگر مسلمان نے اپنا کتا شکار پر چھوڑا اور تسمیہ پڑھا پس کتے نے شکار پکڑا اور اس کو مار کر بہت کر دیا پھر اس کو مار کر مارڈالا تو یہ شکر کھایا جائے گا اور ایسے ہی جبکہ دو کتے چھوڑے پس ایک نے شکار کوست کر دیا ( کہ بھاگ نہ سکے ) پھر دوسرے نے اس کو قتل کر دیا تو کھایا جائے گا اس لئے کہ زخمی کرنے کے بعد زخمی کرنے سے رکنا تعلیم کے تحت داخل نہیں ہے تو اسکو معاف قرار دیا جائے گا۔ تشریح مسلمان نے شکار پر اپنا کتا چھوڑا اس نے شکار پکڑا اور اس کو مار کرست کر دیا کہ بھاگ نہ سکے پھر دوسری مرتبہ اس کو مار ڈالا تو یہ شکار حلال ہے اسی طرح مسلمان نے دو کتے شکار پر چھوڑے اور ایک نے اس کو مار کرست کر دیا اور دوسرے نے اس کو مار ڈالا اب بھی یہ شکار کھایا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ کتے کو یہ تعلیم نہیں دی جاسکتی کہ جب اس کو زخمی کر دے تو نہ مارے بلکہ مالک کا انتظار کرے کہ وہ آ کر اس کو ذبح کرے تا کہ حلال ہو جائے تو جب یہ تعلیم کے تحت داخل نہیں ہو سکتا تو اس کو عذر شمار کر کے معاف قرار دیا جائیگا۔
And if a Muslim sends his dog on game and says the tasmiyah, and it catches it, strikes it and immobilizes it, then strikes it again and kills it, it is eaten. Likewise, if he sends two dogs, and one immobilizes it and the other kills it, it is eaten, because refraining from wounding after wounding does not enter under training, so it is made excusable. Translation: And if a Muslim sent his dog on the game and recited the tasmiyah, then the dog caught the game and struck it and stunned it, then struck it and killed it, then this game will be eaten. And similarly, when two dogs are sent, then one stunned the game (so it couldn't flee), then the other killed it, then it will be eaten, because stopping after wounding is not included under training, so it is considered excused. Explanation: A Muslim sent his dog on the game, it caught the game and struck it and stunned it so it couldn't flee, then a second time it struck and killed it—this game is halal. Similarly, a Muslim sent two dogs on the game, and one stunned it and the other killed it—still this game will be eaten. Because a dog cannot be trained that when it wounds, it should not kill but wait for the owner to come and slaughter it so it becomes halal. Since this cannot be included under training, it is considered an excuse and excused.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with a rational proof. The situation: a Muslim sends a trained dog, which after tasmiyah catches the game and first immobilizes it (wakahadh), then kills it. The game is halal. The same applies if two dogs from the same owner are sent: one immobilizes, the other kills. The proof is that it is impossible to train a dog to stop after wounding and wait for the owner to perform dhabihah. Since this cannot be part of the dog's training, any such action is excused (afu). The ruling is based on necessity and practicality, as the dog's instinct after catching the prey may lead to immediate killing. The Hanafi school therefore considers the game as lawfully killed if the dog was sent with tasmiyah, regardless of the subsequent actions that are beyond the dog's training.

Key Terms
ست کرنا / وقه (wakahadh / st karna): To immobilize or stun the game so it cannot flee.
عفو (afw): Excusable; legal concession allowing the game to be halal despite the dog killing after immobilization.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the game is already dead before the dog kills it.
D) Because the imam has allowed it.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school excuses the dog's kill after immobilization because refraining from killing after wounding is not part of what can be trained.

Ownership of Game When Two Dogs Are Sent: The First Immobilizer's Right
Ruling on ownership and permissibility for two-dog joint hunting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ownership and permissibility when two dogs immobilize and kill game, and identify the key condition that changes the ruling.

Core Idea
The first dog that immobilizes the game owns it; second's killing is excused because its sending was upon the game.

In joint hunting, disputes may arise over who owns the game and whether it is halal. This lesson addresses a specific scenario where two dogs from two owners are sent, one immobilizes the game, and the other kills it. The Hanafi school provides a clear ruling based on the concept of saydiyyah and the timing of the dogs' actions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and actions
Two Muslim owners each send their dog to hunt. One dog immobilizes the game (stuns it), the other kills it.
Immobilization means the game is captured but still alive.
2
Determine ownership rule
The game belongs to the owner of the first dog that immobilized it because that dog removed it from the state of wild game (saydiyyah).
Ownership follows the act that ends the game's wild status, not the kill.
3
Address permissibility despite second dog's kill
The game is halal because the second dog's action is excused—it cannot be trained to refrain from killing after immobilization. Moreover, permissibility is judged at the time of sending, which was upon the game.
This applies even if the owner of the first dog intended to slaughter it.
4
Note the exception
If the second dog was sent after the first had already removed the game from saydiyyah (i.e., after immobilization), the sending is no longer upon the game, and the kill makes the game haram.
The key distinction is the timing of the second dog's sending relative to immobilization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Two Muslim persons have two dogs; one stunned the game, the other killed it, so the game is halal. 'And if two men each send a dog, and one of them immobilizes it and the other kills it, it is eaten, because of what we have said.' Translation: If two persons sent their respective dogs (i.e., Muslims), then one of them stunned the game and the other killed it, then it will be eaten, due to the same reason we have mentioned. Explanation: Two Muslims have two dogs, and both sent them both on the game. Then one dog stunned it, and the other killed it. This is halal because, as per the earlier statement, it is considered excused. But upon this arises a question: whose property is this game? So it is the property of the owner of the first dog, because the first dog removed it from the state of being game. The author says: 'And ownership belongs to the first, because the first removed it from the boundary of being game. However, the sending from the second occurred upon the game, and in permissibility and prohibition, the state at the time of sending is considered, so it is not prohibited. Explanation: He said that this game is owned by the owner of the first dog, because the first dog removed it from being game. Question: When the first dog removed it from being game, and it had not killed it, then obligatory dhabihah became necessary for the owner. When the optional dhabihah could not be performed and it died from the second dog's killing before that, shouldn't it be prohibited? Answer: The second also sent his dog upon the game, and in permissibility and prohibition, the state at the time of sending is considered, so the game will not be prohibited. Also, this is an excuse because it cannot be included under training, so it will be excused. However, if the second sent his dog when the first dog had already removed it from being game, and it came and killed it, then the game will not be halal because the sending was not upon the game.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a combination of a ruling and a subsequent discussion about ownership and permissibility. The initial ruling states that if two Muslims each send a dog, and one immobilizes the game and the other kills it, the game is halal, based on the same excuse as in the previous lesson (the action is not trainable). The author then addresses a practical question: to whom does the game belong? The answer is that ownership belongs to the owner of the first dog because that dog removed the animal from the state of being wild game (saydiyyah), meaning it was captured and under human control. However, a potential issue arises: if the first dog only immobilized but didn't kill, the owner should perform dhabihah to make it halal. But the second dog killed it, so is it still halal? The answer is yes, because the second dog's sending was also upon the game, and permissibility is judged at the time of sending. Additionally, the dog's action is excused because it cannot be trained to refrain from killing. But if the second dog was sent after the first had already taken the game out of the state of being game (i.e., after it was fully under control), then the sending is no longer upon the game, and the killing is not excused, so the game becomes haram. This distinction is crucial for understanding the boundaries of ownership and permissibility.

Key Terms
صیدیت (saydiyyah): The state of being wild game that is permissible to hunt.
ذکاۃ اختیاری (dhabihah ikhtiyariyyah): Optional slaughter; the ritual slaughter that the owner should perform when the game is captured alive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The second dog's owner is a non-Muslim.
C) The game is still alive after immobilization.
D) The first dog did not kill the game.

Key Takeaway
Ownership of game follows the dog that immobilizes (first to remove from saydiyyah), and permissibility depends on whether the second dog's sending occurred upon the game before removal.

Hunting by Shooting: The Role of Intention and Sensed Movement
Introduces rules for hunting by shooting, focusing on cases when hunter hears movement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on game killed when the hunter only sensed movement, and identify the key condition that determines permissibility.

Core Idea
A hunter's intention must be to hunt wild game; if he aimed at a non-game target, the game he kills is not halal.

In this lesson, we examine a common hunting scenario: the hunter hears a sound or senses movement in the field, shoots, and finds game dead. Is it halal to eat? The answer depends not only on what was hit, but also on what the hunter intended to hit. The Hanafi school provides detailed guidance based on the distinction between game and non-game animals, emphasizing the importance of intention (niyyah) and the definition of hunting (istiyad).

Guided Path
1
Identify the key scenario
The hunter senses movement (aahat) in a field without clear sight. He then shoots an arrow or sends a dog, and the animal is caught dead.
This scenario raises the question: is the game halal?
2
Distinguish two cases: hitting the sensed target vs hitting another
If the arrow hits exactly the animal whose movement was sensed, look at that animal's type. If it hits a different animal, look at the type of the originally sensed animal.
The ruling branches based on what was hit versus what was sensed.
3
Determine the nature of the sensed object
Check whether the sensed animal is wild game (washi, e.g., deer, rabbit) or non-game (human, domestic animal like a tame deer or chicken).
If non-game, the hunter's action is not considered istiyad (hunting).
4
Apply the ruling
If the sensed object is game, the killed game (whether the same or a different one) is halal (if edible) or permissible for skin (if non-edible). If the sensed object is not game, the killed game is not halal because the action lacked the intention to hunt a wild animal.
Intention (niyyah) is essential for valid istiyad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الرمي ترجمہ فصل ہے تیر پھینکنے کے بیان میں تشریح اس فصل کو مؤخر کرنے کی وجہ ہم فصل اول میں بیان کر چکے ہیں۔ تنبیه - کبھی شکاری کھیت وغیرہ میں کچھ آہٹ یا حرکت محسوس کرتا ہے پھر وہاں تیر مارتا ہے یا اپنا کتا چھوڑتا ہے اور شکار پکڑ لیا جاتا ہے حالانکہ وہ تیر سے یا کتے کے پکڑنے سے مرچکا ہے تو اب کیا حکم ہے تفصیل اس کی یہ ہے کہ اگر اس نے جن کی آہٹ سنی تھی بعینہ اس کا تیر اس کا لگا ہے تو اسے دیکھا جائے کہ یہ شکار ہے یا نہیں اگر وہ شکار ہے تو اس پر وہی حکم جاری ہوگا جو اس شکار پر ہوا کرتا ہے یعنی اگر ماکول اللحم ہے تو اس کو کھایا جائے گا اور اگر ماکول اللحم نہیں ہے جیسے شیر وغیرہ تو ان کی کھال مباح ہوگی اور اگر جس کی آہٹ سنی تھی تیر اس کو نہیں لگا بلکہ دوسرے شکار کو لگا ہے تو اب دیکھا جائے کہ جس کی آہٹ سنی تھی وہ شکار ہے یا نہیں اگر وہ شکار ہو تو یہ دوسرا شکار جو تیر سے مرا ہے مباح ہے اور اگر جس کی آہٹ سی تھی وہ شکار نہ ہو بلکہ انسان ہو یا پالتو جانور ہو جیسے پلا ہوا ہرن یا مرغی وغیرہ تو جو شکار مرا ہے وہ بھی حلال نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ اب اس کا فعل اصطیار نہیں ہوا کیونکہ اصطیار کہتے ہیں کہ وحشی جانور کا شکار کیا جائے ظاہر الروایہ میں جس شکار کی آہٹ محسوس
