آہٹ (aahat): Sound or movement sensed by the hunter without clear sight.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the permissibility of game killed by a projectile depends on the hunter's intention to hunt a wild animal; shooting at a non-game target invalidates the hunt even if game is hit.

Ownership of Prey When Two Dogs Are Sent Sequentially
Ruling on ownership when two dogs are sent sequentially

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on who owns the prey when two hunting dogs are sent one after the other and explain how the timing of the second dog's release affects the permissibility of the meat.

Core Idea
Ownership follows the first dog's removal from wild state; permissibility depends on timing of second dog's sending.

This lesson examines a nuanced scenario in hunting law: when two trained dogs are sent after prey one after the other. The first dog may only wound or drive the prey, and the second finishes it. Who owns the prey? Is the meat halal? The Hanafi school provides a clear answer based on the legal definition of 'game' (sayd) and the concept of 'removal from the wild state' (khuruj 'an hadd al-saydiyyah). We will unpack the ruling and its underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal question
The scenario: two trained hunting dogs are sent sequentially. The first dog removes the prey from its wild state (e.g., by wounding it) but does not kill it. The second dog then kills it. The key questions: Who owns the prey? Is the meat halal?
The focus is on the timing of the second dog's release relative to the prey's state.
2
Understand the key concepts
Define 'sayd' (wild game), 'saydiyyah' (the state of being wild and unowned), and 'irsal' (the act of releasing the dog). In Hanafi law, once an animal is removed from the wild state (e.g., by being wounded or cornered), it becomes subject to ownership and requires proper slaughter (dhakat ikhtiyariyya).
The moment of 'khuruj an hadd al-saydiyyah' (exit from game status) is critical.
3
Apply the rule to the first dog's action
The first dog's action (even if only wounding) removes the prey from the wild state. Thus, ownership (milk) is established for the owner of the first dog. This is because the prey is no longer free and unattached.
The text explicitly states 'wal-milku lil-awwal' (ownership is for the first).
4
Evaluate the second dog's sending
The permissibility (halal/haram) of the meat depends on the state of the prey at the time the second dog was sent (irsal). If the prey was still in the wild state at that moment, the second dog's kill is valid hunting. If the prey had already been removed from the wild state by the first dog, then the second dog's sending is not upon game, and the meat becomes haram.
The principle: 'wa al-mu'tabaru fi al-ibahati wal-hurmati hal al-irsal' (consideration in permissibility and prohibition is the state at the time of sending).
5
Distinguish the sub-case and conclude
If the second dog was sent after the first dog had already removed the prey from the wild state (e.g., by a wound), then the meat is haram. If the second dog was sent while the prey was still wild (e.g., the first dog only chased but did not wound), the meat is halal. In both cases, ownership remains with the first dog's owner.
The objection about voluntary slaughter is resolved: the first dog's action made dhakat wajib, but the second dog's valid kill overrides that, and the meat is considered properly hunted due to the training of the dogs and the principle of 'afw (pardon).

And ownership is for the first because the first removed it from the limit of being game, except that the sending from the second occurred upon the game, and what is considered in permissibility and prohibition is the state of sending, so it did not become prohibited, contrary to the case when the sending from the second is after the removal from the state of being game by the wounding of the first dog. Translation: And the ownership is for the first, because the first removed it from the limit of being game, but the sending from the second occurred upon the game, and in permissibility and prohibition the state of sending is considered, so it will not be prohibited, contrary to the case when the sending from the second is after the removal from the state of being game due to the first dog's wounding. Commentary: He said that this game is owned by the owner of the first dog because the first dog removed it from being game. Objection: When the first dog removed it from being game but did not kill it, then voluntary slaughter (dhakat ikhtiyariyya) became obligatory upon the owner, and when voluntary slaughter could not be performed and it died from the second dog's killing before that, then it should be prohibited? Answer: The second also released his dog upon the game, and in permissibility and prohibition, the state of sending is considered, so the game will not be prohibited. Also, there is this excuse that it cannot be included under training, so it will be pardoned. However, if the second released his dog when the first dog had removed it from the state of being game, and it came and killed it, then the game will not be permissible because the sending is not upon the game.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hidayah, explained in Urdu commentary. It addresses the scenario where two trained hunting dogs are sent after prey sequentially. The first dog drives the prey out of its wild state (saydiyyah), making it subject to ownership. Even if the first dog does not kill it, the prey is considered as having been taken out of the wild state, so a voluntary slaughter (dhakat ikhtiyariyya) becomes necessary. However, if the second dog then kills it, the question arises whether it becomes carrion (haram). The ruling is that it remains halal because the consideration for permissibility is the state at the time the second dog was sent: if the second dog was sent while the prey was still wild (before the first dog's wounding had fully removed it from saydiyyah), then its sending is valid hunting and the meat is halal. But if the second dog was sent after the first had already removed it from the wild state, then the second dog's sending is not upon game (since it is no longer wild), and the kill is not considered valid hunting, so the meat is prohibited. The commentary uses the principle that the legal consideration is given to the time of sending (irsal) for permissibility and prohibition. The hypothetical objection about the necessity of voluntary slaughter is answered by the fact that the second dog's sending was valid, so the prey is considered properly hunted. Also, since the dogs are trained, there is an element of forgiveness ('afw) because the situation is covered under the rules of trained hunting animals. The key takeaway is that ownership follows the first dog's action of removing from the wild state, but permissibility depends on the timing of the second dog's release relative to that removal.

Key Terms
صید (sayd): Game, prey; wild animal that is hunted.
ارسال (irsal): Sending; specifically, the act of releasing a trained hunting dog or bird at the prey.
صیدیہ (saydiyyah): The state or condition of being wild game, i.e., not owned and free.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where a first dog drives prey out of the wild state but does not kill it, and a second dog kills it, who owns the prey according to the Hanafi ruling?
A) The owner of the second dog, because it killed it.
B) The owner of the first dog, because it removed it from the wild state.
C) The prey belongs to no one; it is considered lost.
D) Both owners share the prey equally.

Key Takeaway
Ownership of hunted prey is determined by which dog first removes it from the wild state, not by which dog kills it; permissibility of the meat depends on whether the second dog was released while the prey was still wild.

Hunting After Hearing a Sound: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on permissibility of prey when a hunter shoots after hearing a sound, with differing opinions of Abu Yusuf and Zufar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of the Sahibayn from the Zahir al-Riwayah on the permissibility of prey when a hunter acts based on a sound, and apply the Hanafi definition of istiyad to determine the halal status.

Core Idea
The permissibility of hunted prey hinges on the act of istiyad, not the specific animal whose sound was heard — but Abu Yusuf and Zufar carve out exceptions.

This lesson delves into a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a common hunting scenario: a hunter hears a sound, shoots, and kills game. The ruling depends on whether the sound originated from a wild animal (game) and whether the act constitutes istiyad. While the Zahir al-Riwayah broadly considers the prey halal, Imams Abu Yusuf and Zufar introduce restrictions based on the nature of the animal that produced the sound. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply the Hanafi framework of istiyad and to appreciate the scope of valid ikhtilaf within the school.

Guided Path
1
Establish the scenario
The hunter hears a sound in a field, shoots an arrow or sends a dog, and the arrow hits and kills an animal. The key question: does the identity of the animal that made the initial sound affect the permissibility of the prey that was actually killed?
Assume the killed animal is otherwise edible (e.g., deer). The initial sound is later determined to be from a different animal.
2
Identify the Zahir al-Riwayah ruling
According to the authoritative transmitted position (Zahir al-Riwayah), the prey is halal as long as the sound was of any wild animal (including swine and predators). The hunter’s intention to hunt and the actual performance of istiyad are sufficient.
The key principle: istiyad does not require the target to be edible meat; it is defined as the pursuit of wild animals.
3
Locate the dissent of Abu Yusuf
Imam Abu Yusuf excludes swine (khinzir) because it is najis al-ayn and absolutely prohibited. If the sound was from a pig, the hunter’s intention is not directed toward permissible game, so the prey is not halal.
Abu Yusuf's reasoning: no part of a pig can ever be permissible, so hunting it is not a valid act of istiyad for permissibility.
4
Locate the dissent of Zufar
Imam Zufar excludes all non-edible meat (ghayr ma'kul al-lahm) because sending a dog or arrow at such animals is not for the purpose of permissibility of consumption (ibaha). Thus, if the sound was from a predator, the prey is not halal.
Zufar restricts the exception to animals whose meat is not eaten, even if they are wild. He does not extend this to Swine (since Swine is also non-edible, but his exception is broader).
5
Analyse the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: In a case where the sound is from a pig, Zahir al-Riwayah says the deer is halal, Abu Yusuf says it is haram, and Zufar says it is halal (since pig is not non-edible? Actually, pig is non-edible, so Zufar would also make it haram. Wait: the text says Zufar excludes non-edible meat. Swine is non-edible, so Zufar would also exclude it. So both Abu Yusuf and Zufar would consider it haram? The text states Abu Yusuf excludes swine specially, Zufar excludes all non-edible, so in the case of pig both exclude. For a predator (like lion), Zufar excludes, Abu Yusuf does not. So the difference between them is on predators: Zahir al-Riwayah says halal; Abu Yusuf says halal; Zufar says haram.
Thamarah: For pig sound, Zahir=halal, Abu Yusuf=haram, Zufar=haram. For lion sound, Zahir=halal, Abu Yusuf=halal, Zufar=haram.

وغیرہ سب داخل ہیں البتہ امام ابو یوسف نے خنزیر کو اور امام زفر نے غیر ماکول اللحم کو خارج کیا۔
Chapter on shooting. Translation: Chapter on the description of shooting. Commentary: The reason for deferring this chapter we have explained in the first chapter. Notification: Sometimes a hunter senses some sound or movement in a field etc., then he shoots an arrow there or releases his dog, and the prey is caught, while it has died from the arrow or the dog's catching. What is the ruling? Its detail is that if the arrow hit exactly the one whose sound he heard, then it is seen whether this is game or not. If it is game, then the same ruling applies as for that game: if it is edible meat, it will be eaten; if it is not edible meat like a lion, etc., then their skin is permissible (mubah). And if the arrow did not hit the one whose sound he heard but hit another game, then it is seen whether the one whose sound he heard is game or not. If it is game, then this other game that died from the arrow is permissible (mubah). And if the one whose sound was heard is not game but a human or a domesticated animal like a tame deer or chicken, etc., then the game that died is also not halal. Because his action is not considered hunting (istiyad), since istiyad is defined as hunting wild animals. In the Zahir al-Riwayah, the game whose sound is sensed includes both edible and non-edible meat, even swine. Therefore, if the sound of swine was heard and he shot an arrow, and that arrow hit some game (other than the swine), that game would be halal. But Imam Abu Yusuf excluded swine due to it being impure in essence (najis al-ayn), so according to him, that game would not be halal. And Imam Zufar specifically excluded non-edible meat, so according to him, if the sound of non-edible meat was heard and he shot and hit some game, it would not be halal. According to the Zahir al-Riwayah, in both cases the game is halal. The proof of Zahir al-Riwayah is that the act (nafl istiyad) should be considered hunting, which in itself is a permissible act; it is not necessary for it to be edible meat. Rather, the criterion is hunting (istiyad), and hunting of non-edible meat also occurs, either for the purpose of skin or to protect from its evil. In any case, in the Hidayah, in one place it says that if the sound heard is of a human, the game is halal, and in another place it says it is haram, which apparently is a contradiction. The reconciliation is that prohibition is when the sound is of a human and the arrow hits some other game, because then his action is not hunting, so the game is not halal. And where it is said to be halal is when the one whose sound was heard is hit by the arrow and dies, and it is game; here his action occurred upon the game, and it is called hunting. Since this is hunting, his assumption is not considered because his assumption was wrong. When the assumption contradicts the action, the action is considered and the assumption is disregarded; hence the game is deemed halal. And whoever hears a sound and thinks it is the sound of game, then shoots an arrow or sends a dog or falcon upon it, and it hits some game, then it becomes clear that it was the sound of game: the hit (prey) is halal, whatever game it may be, because he intended hunting. And from Abu Yusuf it is narrated that he specifically excluded swine from that due to the severity of prohibition; do you not see that permissibility is not established in any part of it, unlike predators because [hunting] affects their skin. And Zufar specifically excluded from it what is not eaten for meat because sending in it is not for permissibility. Translation: And whoever hears a sound and assumes it to be the sound of game, then shoots an arrow or sends a dog or falcon upon it, and it hits some game, then it becomes clear that it was the sound of game: the hit (prey) is halal, whatever game it may be, because he intended hunting. And from Abu Yusuf it is narrated that he specifically excluded swine from that due to the severity of prohibition; do you not see that permissibility is not established in any part of it, unlike predators because [hunting] affects their skin. And Zufar specifically excluded from it what is not eaten for meat because sending in it is not for permissibility. Commentary: All this discussion is when a sound is heard and the arrow hits another game; in that case, it is sufficient that the sound heard is absolutely game, so that the act of hunting can occur. Hence, in the Zahir al-Riwayah, lions and swine etc. are all included. However, Imam Abu Yusuf excluded swine, and Imam Zufar excluded non-edible meat.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage handles the ruling when a hunter hears a sound, thinks it is game, and shoots or sends his dog, but the arrow hits a different prey. The central principle is that the permissibility of the prey depends on whether the hunter's action constitutes hunting (istiyad). Istiyad is defined as the pursuit of wild animals. According to the Zahir al-Riwayah, if the sound heard is that of any wild animal (including swine and predators), the hit prey is halal because the hunter intended hunting, and the actual outcome does not invalidate the act. Imam Abu Yusuf narrows this by excluding swine (khinzir) because it is najis al-ayn (impure in essence) and absolutely prohibited; thus, if the hunter aimed thinking it was swine, his intention was not toward permissible game, so the hit prey is not halal. Imam Zufar excludes all non-edible meat (ghayr ma'kul al-lahm) because hunting such animals is not done for food but for other purposes (skin or protection), so the sending is not for permissibility (ibaha) of consumption. The commentary also reconciles an apparent contradiction in the Hidayah: one place says if the sound is of a human, the prey is halal; another says haram. The resolution is that if the hunter hits the human (thinking it was game), the prey (the human) is not game, but if he hits another game, it is halal only if the sound was of game. If the sound was of a human, then hitting another game is not istiyad because the intended target was not wild, so the action is not valid hunting. The key takeaway is that the ruling hinges on the nature of the sound heard and the hunter's intention, with disagreement among later Hanafi scholars about the extent of inclusion.

Key Terms
اصطياد (istiyad): Hunting; the act of pursuing and capturing wild game.
اباحة (ibaha): Permissibility; specifically, the legal permissibility of consuming the meat of a hunted animal.
مخنزیر (khinzir): Swine, pig; considered najis al-ayn (impure in essence) in Islamic law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Zahir al-Riwayah, if a hunter hears a sound, thinks it is a wild animal, shoots, and kills a deer, but later realizes the sound was from a pig, what is the ruling on the deer?
A) The deer is haram because the hunter intended to shoot a pig.
B) The deer is halal because the hunter intended hunting and the sound was of game.
C) The deer is halal only if the pig was also hunted.
D) The deer is considered carrion.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of prey when a hunter shoots after a sound depends on whether the sound was from a wild animal; the Zahir al-Riwayah includes all wild animals, while Abu Yusuf and Zufar carve out exceptions based on the status of the animal whose sound was heard.

The Zahir al-Riwayah Proof: Hunting Includes Non-Edible Game
Rational proof for general hunting ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hunting non-edible animals and explain the rational proof that the term hunting is general.

Core Idea
Hunting is defined by the act, not the edibility of the prey — permissibility of use depends on the animal's capacity.

This passage presents the core rational proof (dalil aqli) from the Zahir al-Riwayah that hunting is not restricted to edible animals. The Hanafi school bases this on the linguistic generality of 'اصطياد' and the concept of 'قبول المحل' (the animal's capacity to accept permissibility). Understanding this proof is essential for correctly applying hunting rules to animals hunted for skin, fur, or pest control.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi claim
The Hanafi school holds that the term 'hunting' (اصطياد) applies to any animal, not just those with edible meat. This is the position being proven.
This contrasts with other schools like the Shafi'i.
2
Understand the rational proof (dalil aqli)
The proof is based on language: the word 'hunting' is general in Arabic, encompassing all wild animals. Therefore, the act itself is permissible (mubah) regardless of edibility.
This is a dalil aqli because it relies on linguistic analysis, not a specific text.
3
Apply the concept of 'qabilah' (capacity)
The permissibility of consuming or using the hunted animal depends on the animal's capacity to accept permissibility (قبول المحل). For example, a lion's skin may be permissible, but its meat is not; a pig's skin and meat are both impermissible because it does not accept permissibility at all.
This explains why the ruling is not a blanket permission for all hunted animals.
4
Analyze the analogy with shooting at one game and hitting another
The passage analogizes: if you shoot at a permissible game and hit another, the hit is permissible. Similarly, if you hunt a non-edible animal, the permissibility depends on whether the action was truly hunting (aiming at a valid target).
This highlights the importance of intention and the nature of the act.
5
Identify exceptions
If the target is not actually a huntable animal (e.g., aiming at a sound that turns out to be human or domesticated animal), the action is not considered hunting, so the accidental hit does not become permissible.
This preserves the distinction between hunting and other acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووجه الظاهر ان اسم الاصطياد لا يختص بالماكول فوقع الفعل اصطياداً وهو فعل مباح في نفسه واباحة التناول ترجع الى المحل فتثبت بقدر ما يقبله لحما وجلدا وقد لا تثبت اذا لم يقبله واذا وقع اصطيادا صار كانه رمی الی صید فاصاب غيره. و لوتبين انه حس آدمی او حیوان اهلى لا يحل المصاب لان الفعل ليس باصطياد والطير الداجن الذى ياوى البيوت اهلى والظبى الموثق بمنزلته لما بينا
The manifest proof is that the term hunting is not specific to edible animals, so the action occurs as hunting, and it is an action permissible in itself. The permissibility of consumption goes back to the substance, so it is established to the extent that it accepts meat and skin, and it may not be established if it does not accept it. When it occurs as hunting, it is as if he shot at a game and hit another. And if it becomes clear that it was the sound of a human or a domesticated animal, the targeted animal does not become permissible because the action is not hunting. The bird that nests in houses is domesticated, and the tied gazelle is like it, for what we have explained.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents the proof of the Zahir al-Riwayah position in the Hanafi school regarding hunting non-edible animals. The key point is that the term 'hunting' (اصطياد) is not restricted to animals whose meat is edible; rather, it applies to any animal that can be hunted for skin, fur, or removal of harm. The act of hunting is permissible in itself (مباح), and the permissibility of utilizing the animal's meat or skin depends on the capacity of that specific animal. For example, if you hunt a lion, its meat may not be permissible but its skin is; if you hunt a pig, neither is permissible because it does not accept permissibility at all. The ruling is analogous to shooting at one game and hitting another: if the intended target is a permissible game, the accidental hit is also permissible. However, if the intended target's sound turns out to be from a human or a domesticated animal, the action was never truly hunting, so the accidental hit remains impermissible. Domesticated birds that nest in houses and tied gazelles are treated as domesticated animals, not wild game.

Key Terms
اصطياد (istiyad): The act of hunting any animal, regardless of edibility.
ماكول اللحم (ma'kul al-lahm): Animals whose meat is deemed edible in Islamic law.
محل (mahall): The animal itself, which 'accepts' permissibility to the extent of its capacity (meat/skin).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Zahir al-Riwayah, if a hunter shoots at a sound and hits a deer, but the sound was actually from a human, what is the ruling on the deer?
A) It is permissible because the action was hunting.
B) It is not permissible because the action was not actually hunting.
C) It is permissible only if the deer is edible.
D) It is permissible if the hunter intended to hunt.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that hunting is defined by the act, not the edibility of the prey; permissibility of use depends on the animal's capacity (qabilah).

Presumptions in Hunting: Bird vs. Camel
Rulings on accidental hits when the intended target's nature is unknown

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of default presumption (zahir al-tawahhush vs. isti'nas) to determine the permissibility of accidental catch in hunting.

Core Idea
A hit game's permissibility depends on the intended target's default nature, not the outcome.

When a hunter aims at a bird but hits another game, and the bird escapes, is the game halal? What if he aimed at a camel? In Hanafi fiqh, the answer hinges on the default state of the intended target. This lesson unpacks the ruling from Al-Hidayah, where birds are presumptively wild and camels are presumptively domestic, and shows how these presumptions govern ambiguous hunting scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two cases: shooting at a bird and shooting at a camel. In both, the projectile hits an unspecified game, and the intended target escapes, leaving doubt about whether it was wild or domestic.
The ruling for each case differs based on the default presumption of the intended target's species.
2
Apply the default presumption
For birds, the presumption is that they are wild (zahir al-tawahhush), because most birds are wild. For camels, the presumption is domestication (isti'nas), because camels are primarily domestic animals.
The default is based on the predominant nature (ghalabah) of the species, not the individual animal's behavior.
3
Derive the ruling for the accidental catch
If the intended target is presumptively wild (bird), the act is considered valid hunting, so the accidentally hit game is halal. If the intended target is presumptively domestic (camel), the act is not considered hunting (since camels are not typically hunted), so the game is haram.
The ruling does not require knowing the actual state of the intended target; the default suffices.
4
Reflect on the underlying principle
This ruling illustrates the Hanafi method of relying on the predominant state (ghalabah) in cases of uncertainty. The default presumption takes the place of certainty until evidence to the contrary appears.
The same principle applies across many fiqh chapters, such as purity of water or permissibility of food.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو رمى الى طائر فاصاب صيدا ومر الطائر ولايدرى وحشى هو اوغير وحشى حل الصيد لان الظاهر فيه التوحش ولو رمى الى بعير فاصاب صيدا ولا يدرى نا دهو ام لا لا يحل الصيد لان الاصل فيه الاستيناس
And if he shot at a bird and it hit a game, and the bird flew away, and it is not known whether it is wild or not, the game is permissible because the presumption is that it is wild. And if he shot at a camel and it hit a game, and it is not known whether it is wild or not, the game is not permissible because the original state of a camel is domestication.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage addresses cases of uncertainty about the intended target's nature. When a hunter shoots at a bird but misses and hits another game, and the original bird escapes, the hit game is considered permissible because birds are presumed wild by default (ظاهر التوحش). This is because most birds are wild, and the original bird's flight indicates it was not a captive. Conversely, if the hunter shoots at a camel and hits a game, the game is not permissible unless it is known that the camel was wild (نادّ). The default for camels is domestication (استيناس), and without evidence that the camel was wild, the original act cannot be considered hunting. This reflects the Hanafi principle of relying on the predominant state (غالب) in cases of ambiguity.

Key Terms
ظاهر التوحش (zahir al-tawahhush): The presumption that an animal is wild by default, used for birds.
استيناس (isti'nas): Domestication; the default state for camels and other typically domestic animals.
نادّ (nadd): A runaway or wild camel; the exception to the default domestic state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a hunter aims at a bird, misses, and hits a rabbit, but the bird is not seen again, what is the ruling on the rabbit?
A) It is permissible because the bird is presumed wild.
B) It is not permissible unless the bird was confirmed wild.
C) It is permissible only if the rabbit is edible.
D) It is not permissible because the act of hunting was incomplete.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi hunting rulings, the intended target's default state (wild vs. domestic) determines the permissibility of accidental catch, not the actual outcome.

Shooting at Fish or Locust: Two Narrations from Abu Yusuf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on accidental hit when target requires no slaughter

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two narrations from Abu Yusuf on this issue and identify the mufta bihi position with its reasoning.

Core Idea
Two narrations from Abu Yusuf: one permits accidental hit on game, the other prohibits it due to absence of dhabh.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute from Imam Abu Yusuf regarding a unique hunting scenario: a hunter shoots at a fish or locust (animals that do not require ritual slaughter, dhabh) but accidentally hits a game animal. The ruling hinges on whether the act is considered valid hunting. By understanding the two narrations and their bases, you will grasp the nuance in Hanafi fiqh where rulings can differ based on underlying rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the two narrations
Note the act: shooting at a fish or locust (no dhabh required) and accidentally hitting game. Two narrations from Abu Yusuf: one permits, one prohibits.
2
Understand the reasoning for each narration
Permitting: because fish and locust are considered game (صيد). Prohibiting: because they have no ritual slaughter (ذكاة), so shooting at them is not a valid hunting act.
3
Determine the mufta bihi position
The first narration (halal) is generally relied upon in the Hanafi school, as the act of hunting is confirmed.
Authority weight: nawadir (rare transmission).
4
Note the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: In the first narration, the accidentally hit game is halal; in the second, it is haram.
5
Reflect on the authority weight
This is a Nawadir transmission from Abu Yusuf, not from Zahir al-Riwayah. Later scholars preferred the permitting narration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو رمى الى سمكة او جرادة فاصاب صيدا يحل في رواية عن ابى يوسف لانه صيد وفي اخرى عنه لا يحل لانه لا ذكاة فيهما
And if he shot at a fish or a locust and it hit a game, it is permissible in one narration from Abu Yusuf because it is game, and in another narration from him it is not permissible because there is no ritual slaughter for them.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute, specifically two conflicting narrations from Imam Abu Yusuf regarding a case where a hunter shoots at a fish or locust (which do not require ritual slaughter, dhabh) but accidentally hits another game animal. In one narration, the hit game is deemed permissible because fish and locust are themselves valid game (صيد), so the act counts as hunting. In the other narration, it is not permissible because fish and locust lack the requirement of dhabh (ذكاة), implying they are not considered proper objects of hunting that would validate the act. The authoritative view (mufta bihi) within the Hanafi school is often the first narration, that it is permissible, based on the principle that the act of hunting is established. The second narration reflects caution due to the absence of dhabh. This shows the nuance in Hanafi fiqh where rulings can differ based on the underlying rationale.

Key Terms
ذكاة (dhakah): Ritual slaughter required for most land animals and birds; not required for fish and locust.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the narration from Abu Yusuf that considers the hit permissible, what is the reason?
A) Because fish and locust are edible.
B) Because fish and locust are considered game (صيد).
C) Because the hunter intended to hunt something.
D) Because the accidental hit is always permissible.

Key Takeaway
When shooting at fish or locust (no dhabh required), the accidental hit on game is permissible according to the relied-upon narration from Abu Yusuf.

Ruling on Arrow Hitting Game After Missing Bird or Camel
Ruling based on presumption of wildness or domestication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether game is halal when an arrow misses its intended target, applying the default presumption of wildness or domestication.

Core Idea
The default nature of the intended target determines the halal status of the game hit.

When a hunter shoots at an animal but the arrow hits another, the ruling depends on the nature of the intended target. The Hanafi school applies the legal principle of al-asl (default presumption) to decide: birds are presumed wild, camels domesticated. This lesson examines the two cases presented in Al-Hidayah and the rational basis for the distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text presents two cases: shooting at a bird and shooting at a camel. Note the intended target in each.
Distinguish between archery at birds vs. camels.
2
State the ruling for each case
For the bird scenario, the hit game is halal; for the camel scenario, it is not halal.
Note the difference: halal vs. not halal.
3
Determine the key legal principle
The rulings rely on the default presumption (al-asl) of the intended animal's nature: wildness for birds, domestication for camels.
The principle is al-zaahir and al-asl.
4
Explain why the default differs
The default for birds is wildness (tawahhush) because most birds are wild; for camels, domestication (isti'nas) because camels are typically tame.
This is based on common observation (urf).
5
Apply the principle to a new case
If the intended target's nature is unknown, the default is applied. For a bird, it is considered wild; for a camel, domesticated.
For example, if shooting at a pigeon, the default is wild.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو رمى الى طائر فاصاب صيدا ومر الطائر ولايدرى وحشى هو اوغير وحشى حل الصيد لان الظاهر فيه التوحش ولو رمى الى بعير فاصاب صيدا ولا يدرى نا دهو ام لا لا يحل الصيد لان الاصل فيه الاستيناس
And if he shot at a bird and it hit game, and the bird passed by and it is not known whether it was wild or not wild, the game is halal because the apparent (default) in it is wildness. And if he shot at a camel and it hit game, and it is not known whether it had become wild or not, the game is not halal because the default in it is domestication.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents two contrasting rulings regarding an arrow that misses its intended target and hits another animal. In the first scenario, a hunter shoots at a bird. The bird escapes, and the arrow hits a different animal. The ruling states that the hit animal is halal because the default presumption (al-asl) concerning birds is that they are wild (tawahhush). Therefore, the hunter's act is considered legitimate hunting, and the animal is lawful. In the second scenario, a hunter shoots at a camel, intending to hunt it, but the arrow hits another animal. Here, the ruling states that the hit animal is not halal because the default presumption concerning camels is that they are domesticated (isti'nas). Since the hunter intended to shoot a non-wild animal, the action is not considered proper hunting (sayd), and the game hit is not permissible. The passage emphasizes that the nature of the intended target determines the validity of hunting.

Key Terms
توحش (tawahhush): The state of being wild (as opposed to domesticated). For birds, the default is wildness.
استيناس (isti'nas): The state of being domesticated (tame). For camels and similar animals, the default is domestication.
اصل (asl): Default presumption or legal principle that governs the case in the absence of evidence to the contrary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A hunter shoots an arrow at a bird, but the bird flies away and the arrow hits a deer. The bird's status (wild or tame) is unknown. According to the Hanafi school, is the deer halal?
A) Yes, because birds are presumed wild unless proven otherwise.
B) No, because the hunter's intention was for the bird, not the deer.
C) Yes, because any animal hit by a hunter's arrow is halal.
D) No, because the bird might be tame.

Key Takeaway
The halal status of game hit by an arrow that misses its intended target hinges on the default presumption (al-asl) of the intended animal's wildness or domestication.

Ruling When Hunter Mistook Target for Human
Ruling that game is halal despite mistaken belief

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a hunter mistakes game for a human and explain why the animal is halal despite the mistaken belief.

Core Idea
Assumption is irrelevant once the actual identity of the target is determined.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario in hunting where the hunter's subjective belief contradicts the objective reality. The Hanafi school provides a clear principle: the actual factual determination overrides the hunter's assumption. Understanding this ruling helps clarify the role of intention versus outcome in the permissibility of game.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
Read the source text carefully: the hunter shoots based on a sound, thinking it is a human, but discovers it is game. The ruling is that the game is halal.
The phrase 'yahillu' explicitly states permissibility.
2
Understand the legal basis
The text says 'lā mu‘tabara bi-ẓannihi' – his assumption (zann) is not given weight. The actual identity (ta‘ayyun) of the target determines the ruling.
This is a core Hanafi principle: in hunting, objective reality prevails over subjective belief when the fact is known.
3
Distinguish from cases where intention matters
Contrast this with situations where the hunter intends to hunt a specific animal but misses, or where the arrow kills a different animal. In this case, the arrow hits exactly the source of the sound, so the act of shooting is effectively a hunt.
The key is that the target turned out to be game – the assumption was about its identity, not about the act.
4
Apply the ruling to similar scenarios
Consider if the hunter had no specific intention (e.g., shooting randomly) and it hits game – also halal. The ruling emphasizes that the hunter's mistaken belief does not render the meat unlawful.
This reinforces the Hanafi position that the permissibility of game depends on the actual animal, not the hunter's state of mind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اصاب المسموع حسه وقد ظنه ادميا فاذا هو صيد يحل لانه لا معتبر بطنه مع تعينه
And if [the arrow] hits the one whose sound was heard, and he thought it was a human, then suddenly it is game, it is halal because his assumption is not considered after its actual identity is determined.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This ruling clarifies a situation where a hunter hears a sound and shoots an arrow at what he thought was a human being, but after hitting, he discovers it was a game animal. The ruling states that the animal is halal because the hunter's assumption (zann) that it was human is disregarded once the actual target is identified as game. The actual action of hunting (sayd) took place, and the intention to hunt a game animal is not required for the animal to be lawful; rather, the act of shooting at the specific target suffices. This ruling might seem contradictory to earlier ones that emphasize the intended target's nature, but the commentary explains that here the arrow hit precisely the source of the sound, so the action is consistent with hunting.

Key Terms
اعتبار (i'tibar): Consideration or weight given to something in legal evaluation. Here, the hunter's assumption is not given weight.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because the deer is actually game.

C) Yes, only if he recited tasmiyah.
D) No, because he committed a sin by shooting at a human.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the permissibility of game depends on the actual animal killed, not the hunter's subjective belief about its identity before the shot.

Requirement of Tasmiyah at the Time of Shooting
Condition of reciting Bismillah when shooting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for halal hunting by shooting and explain the qiyas that establishes the obligation of tasmiyah.

Core Idea
Tasmiyah at the moment of shooting is wajib; deliberate omission makes the game haram.

The author of Al-Hidayah extends the rule of tasmiyah from releasing a hunting animal to shooting an arrow. The arrow functions as an instrument of slaughter, just like a knife, so the same condition applies. Additionally, the arrow must cause a wound to effect a valid 'expedient slaughter.' This lesson analyzes the conditions, the underlying qiyas, and the practical consequences of omission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is shooting an arrow (or any projectile) at game. The ruling: the game is halal to eat if two conditions are met.
2
List the two conditions
Condition 1: Reciting tasmiyah at the moment of shooting. Condition 2: The arrow must wound the animal.
Both are necessary.
3
Understand the basis (ta'lil)
The author gives a rational proof (dalīl ʿaqlī): shooting is a form of slaughter because the arrow is the instrument (ālah) of killing, just like a knife. Therefore, tasmiyah is required as it is for regular slaughter.
Qiyas: shooting is analogous to slaughter (dhabh).
4
Apply the ruling to a case of omission
If the hunter deliberately omits tasmiyah, the game is not halal, even if the arrow wounds and kills.
The text explicitly says deliberate omission invalidates.
5
Note the expansion from irsāl to ramī
The same rule that applied to releasing a trained animal (irsāl) now applies to shooting (ramī). This expands the coverage of the tasmiyah requirement.
This is the 'expansion probe' nature of the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و اذا سمى الرجل عند الرمي اكل ما اصاب اذا جرح السهم فمات لانه ذابح بالرمى لكون السهم الة له فتشترط التسمية عنده وجميع البدن محل لهذا النوع من الذكاة ولا بد من الجرح ليتحقق معنى الذكاة على ما بيناه
And if a man recites tasmiyah at the time of shooting, then he may eat what the arrow hits if the arrow wounds and it dies, because he is slaughtering by shooting, since the arrow is a tool for him, so tasmiyah is required at its time. And the entire body is a place for this type of slaughter, and wounding is necessary to realize the meaning of slaughter (dhabh), as we have explained.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage establishes the rule that reciting 'Bismillah' (tasmiyah) is a prerequisite for the validity of hunting by shooting (ramī), just as it is for hunting by releasing a trained animal (irsāl). The reason is that the arrow functions as the instrument of slaughter (ālah al-dhabh). The entire body of the animal is a permissible location for this type of expedient slaughter (dhabh iḍṭirārī), unlike normal slaughter which requires the throat. However, it is necessary that the arrow causes a wound (jarḥ) to validate the act of slaughter. This is consistent with the earlier discussion on irsāl.

Key Terms
تسمية (tasmiyah): Reciting 'Bismillah' (In the name of Allah) at the moment of shooting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A hunter shoots an arrow at a deer without reciting tasmiyah. The arrow wounds the deer, and it dies. According to the Hanafi school, is the deer halal?
A) Yes, because the arrow caused a wound.
B) No, because tasmiyah was omitted at shooting.
C) Yes, because he can recite tasmiyah after shooting.
D) No, because the deer was not properly slaughtered.

Key Takeaway
The obligatory recitation of tasmiyah at the moment of shooting is a condition for the game to be halal, based on the analogy between the arrow and a slaughter knife.

Ruling on Shooting Game Mistaken for a Human
Ruling with proof: game halal despite mistaken human intent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a hunter mistakenly shoots game thinking it is a human and apply the legal principle that assumption is not considered when reality is established.

Core Idea
Assumption yields to reality: game is halal despite mistaken human-intent.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a specific hunting scenario where the hunter's assumption conflicts with reality. The key legal principle is that subjective assumptions have no legal weight once the true nature of the target is known. Understanding this ruling requires recognizing how the Hanafi school prioritizes objective reality over subjective intent in certain contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The act is shooting at an animal that the hunter thought was a human but was actually game. The ruling: the game is halal (mubah). Identify that the hunter's intention (to shoot a human) is overridden by the actual identity of the target.
The ruling is from the main text of Al-Hidayah, thus from Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Locate the Evidence
The author cites the maxim 'لا معتبر بظنه مع تعينه' (his assumption is not considered when the reality is established). Identify this as the proof (dalil).
This maxim is a legal principle, not a direct scriptural text.
3
Extract the Key Legal Maxim
The maxim 'لا معتبر بظنه' means an assumption has no legal consideration when the actual state of affairs is known. Understand that this principle applies to scenarios where a person's mistaken subjective belief conflicts with objective reality.
This maxim is part of broader Hanafi usul, often applied in rulings where actuality overrides presumption.
4
Apply the Maxim to the Scenario
Apply the maxim: since the target is actually game (reality), the hunter's assumption (that it was a human) is disregarded. Therefore, the act is considered hunting (اصطیاد), which is permissible, and the game becomes halal.
The action is considered, not the mistaken thought.
5
Note the Apparent Contradiction and Its Resolution
The commentary mentions that this ruling seems to contradict an earlier one. The resolution is that the earlier case involved different facts (likely where the hunter intended something else). Understand that the principle of weighing reality over assumption resolves the apparent conflict.
This step is important for avoiding confusion when studying multiple related rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جس کی آہٹ سنی تھی بعینہ تیر اس کو لگا حالانکہ اس کو انسان گمان کرتا تھا جبکہ وہ شکار ہے تو وہ شکار حلال ہے
ولو اصاب المسموع حسه وقد ظنه ادميا فاذا هو صيد يحل لانه لا معتبر بظنه مع تعينه
ترجمہ اور اگر تیر اس شکار کو لگا ہو جس کی آہٹ سنی تھی حالانکہ اس نے اس کو آدمی گمان کیا تھا پس اچانک وہ شکار ہے تو وہ حلال ہے اس لئے کہ اس کے گماں کا اعتبار نہیں کیا جائے گا شکار کے متعین ہونے کے بعد ۔
تشریح جس کی آہٹ سنی تھی بعینہ اس کو تیر مارا حالانکہ وہ اس کو انسان گمان کیے ہوئے تھا حالانکہ وہ شکار ہے اور تیر اس کو لگا تو وہ حلال ہو گیا اور یہاں اس کے فعل اور گمان میں تعارض ہے تو گمان کو ملغیٰ قرار دیا گیا اور فعل کا اعتبار کیا گیا ہے اور فعل اصطیاد ہے لہذا شکار حلال ہوگا یہ بظاہر پہلے مسئلے سے متعارض ہے اس کا تفصیلی جواب ہم ماقبل میں بیان کر چکے ہیں۔
And if the arrow hits the one whose sound was heard and he thought it was a human, but it turns out to be game, it is permissible because his assumption is not considered when the reality is established. Translation: And if the arrow hits this game whose sound was heard, even though he thought it was a human, then suddenly it is game, it is permissible because his assumption is not considered after the game is identified. Commentary: The one whose sound was heard, exactly that one is hit by the arrow, even though he thought it was a human, but it is game and the arrow hit it, so it becomes halal. Here his action and assumption conflict; the assumption is disregarded and the action (hunting) is considered, therefore the game is halal. This apparently contradicts an earlier ruling, but we have explained its detailed answer previously.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling regarding hunting with an arrow: if a hunter shoots at an animal that he mistakenly thought was a human, the game is halal. The proof is the legal maxim that assumption is not given weight when reality is established. The author notes that this ruling seems to contradict a previous one, but the reconciliation has already been provided.

Key Terms
المسموع حسه (al-masmu' hissuhu): The one whose sound was heard (the target animal).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The game is haram because he intended to shoot a human.
B) The game is halal because the actual target is game, and his assumption is disregarded.
C) The game is halal only if he said tasmiyah.
D) The game is makruh because of the mistake.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the actual nature of the target (game or not) determines permissibility, overriding the hunter's mistaken assumption.

Necessity of Tasmiyah at the Time of Shooting (Rami)
Ruling that tasmiyah is required when shooting an arrow

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tasmiyah during rami and explain its proof using qiyas to irsal.

Core Idea
Saying Bismillah at the moment of shooting is wajib; intentional omission makes the game haram.

In hunting, the moment of releasing the tool is crucial. Just as the hunter must say tasmiyah when sending a dog, he must say it when shooting an arrow. This ruling is derived by qiyas (analogy) and is part of the broader category of emergency slaughter (dhakah idtirari).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the act is rami (shooting an arrow at game). The ruling is that saying tasmiyah at the time of shooting is wajib; if omitted intentionally, the game is not halal.
This ruling is parallel to the requirement for irsal (sending a dog).
2
Locate the dalil
The proof for this ruling is qiyas (analogy) to irsal, which is established by the Sunnah. The text states: 'because he is a slaughterer by shooting, since the arrow is his tool'.
The dalil is zanni al-thubut but qat'i al-dalala according to Hanafi usul.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Identify the effective cause ('illa): both irsal and rami involve releasing a tool that kills the game. Therefore, the same condition (tasmiyah) applies at the moment of release.
The commentary explicitly equates the two under dhabh idtirari.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some scholars differentiate between irsal and rami, but the Hanafi school equates them. The Shafi'i school also requires tasmiyah but considers it a fard, not wajib (though this distinction is not explored here).
The source does not elaborate on intra-madhhab or inter-madhhab differences, but the reliance on qiyas is clear.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The position that tasmiyah is wajib at the time of shooting is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position from Zahir al-Riwayah.
This is consistent with the general Hanafi principle that a condition (shart) for validity must be fulfilled at the specific moment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تسمیہ جس طرح ارسال کے لئے ضروری ہے اسی طرح رمی کے لئے بھی ضروری ہے
و اذا سمى الرجل عند الرمي اكل ما اصاب اذا جرح السهم فمات لانه ذابح بالرمى لكون السهم الة له فتشترط التسمية عنده وجميع البدن محل لهذا النوع من الذكاة ولا بد من الجرح ليتحقق معنى الذكاة على ما بيناه
ترجمہ اور جب انسان نے پھینکنے کے وقت تسمیہ پڑھا تو جس کو وہ تیر لگا اس کو کھایا جائے گا جبکہ تیر نے زخمی کر دیا ہو پس شکار مر جائے اس لئے کہ یہ (رامی) تیر پھینکنے کے ذریعہ ذبح کرنے والا ہے تیر کے اس کے لئے آلہ ہونے کی وجہ سے تو تیر پھینکنے کے وقت تسمیہ شرط ہے اور پورا بدن ذکاۃ کی اس نوع کا محل ہے اور ضروری ہے زخم لگا دینا تا کہ ذکاۃ کے معنی متحقق ہو سکیں اس تفصیل کے مطابق جس کو ہم بیان کر چکے ہیں۔
تشریح جس طرح ارسال کے وقت تسمیہ ضروری تھا اسی طرح رمی کے وقت ضروری ہے جیسے ارسال آلہ ذبح ہے ایسے ہی تیر پھینکنا بھی آلہ ذبح ہے اور ارسال ورمی ذکاۃ اضطراری کے تحت داخل ہے اور ذکاۃ اضطراری میں بدن کا کوئی حصہ معین نہیں ہے بلکہ جہاں بھی لگ جائے خواہ تیر آنکھ میں گھس جائے یا چوتڑ میں سب برابر ہے البتہ زخم کرنا شرط ہے جس کی تفصیل ارسال کے بیان میں گذر چکی ہے۔
Tasmiyah is as necessary for shooting as it is for sending. And if a man says tasmiyah at the time of shooting, he may eat what the arrow hits if the arrow wounds it and it dies, because he is a slaughterer by shooting, since the arrow is his tool. So tasmiyah is a condition at the time of shooting. And the entire body is a place for this type of slaughter, but a wound is necessary to actualize the meaning of slaughter, according to what we have explained. Commentary: Just as tasmiyah is necessary at the time of sending (the hunting dog), similarly it is necessary at the time of shooting. Just as sending is a tool of slaughter, similarly shooting an arrow is a tool of slaughter. Both sending and shooting fall under emergency slaughter (dhabh idtirari). In emergency slaughter, no specific part of the body is specified; wherever the arrow enters, whether the eye or the buttocks, it is the same. However, wounding is a condition, as detailed in the section on sending.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage establishes that saying tasmiyah (Bismillah) at the time of shooting an arrow is a condition for the game to be halal. The reasoning is analogical (qiyas) to sending a hunting dog. In both cases, the tool (arrow or dog) causes the death, so tasmiyah at the moment of releasing the tool is required. Additionally, a wound (jarh) is necessary because the animal must die from the wound to be considered properly slaughtered. The entire body is valid for this type of slaughter (dhabh idtirari), unlike normal slaughter where the neck is specified.

Key Terms
ذکاۃ اضطراری (dhakah idtirari): Emergency slaughter; a method of legal slaughter that does not require a cut to the neck, but the animal dies by a wound from a tool.
رمی (rami): Shooting or throwing an arrow, spear, or bullet at game.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is tasmiyah required in hunting with an arrow?
A) At the time of slaughtering the game.
B) At the time of shooting the arrow.
C) At the time of finding the game.
D) Tasmiyah is not required for hunting.

Key Takeaway
Tasmiyah at the moment of releasing the hunting tool is wajib; intentional omission renders the game haram.

Slaughter of Game Found Alive
Ruling that game hit by arrow must be slaughtered if found alive

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that game found alive after being shot must be slaughtered and identify the consequence of omission.

Core Idea
If game is found alive after being hit, slaughter is obligatory; without it, the meat is haram.

This concise passage from Al-Hidayah delivers a critical hunting regulation: when a hunter recovers game that is still alive after being struck, he must perform proper Islamic slaughter (dhabh). The author references a more detailed earlier discussion, but the core principle is clear and binding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the recovery of game alive after shooting. The ruling is that dhabh (slaughter) becomes obligatory (wajib). Determine the exact hukm level from the text: the word 'lazim' indicates obligation.
In Hanafi fiqh, 'lazim' often implies a wajib ruling.
2
State the consequence of omission
The text explicitly states that if slaughter is not performed, the meat will not be halal. This establishes a direct consequence: without dhabh, the game remains haram.
3
Recognize the authority reference
The author (Quduri) mentions that this issue has been fully explained in the first chapter. This signals that the current passage is a summary of a detailed earlier ruling, which should be consulted for complete conditions and differences.
This is an example of an 'expansion probe' – the text points to a fuller discussion elsewhere.
4
Apply the ruling to a concrete scenario
Imagine a hunter shoots a deer with an arrow and finds it alive. He must immediately perform dhabh by cutting the throat. If he delays until it dies, the meat becomes haram.

Source Text

اگر شکار زندہ مل جائے ذبح لازم ہے ورنہ حلال نہ ہو گا
قال فان ادركه حيا ذكاه وقد بيناها بوجوهها والاختلاف فيها في الفصل الاول فلا نعيده.
ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا پس اگر اس نے (رامی نے) شکار کو زندہ پالیا تو اس کو ذبح کرے اور ہم اس مسئلہ کو مع اس کی تمام صورتوں کے اور اس میں اختلاف کو پہلی فصل میں بیان کر چکے ہیں پس ہم اس کا اعادہ نہیں کریں گے۔
تشریح اگر تیر پھینکنے والے کو شکار زندہ مل جائے تو اب ذبح کرنا لازم ہے ورنہ حلال نہ ہوگا مصنف نے پہلی فصل میں ص ۴۸۰ ج ۴ پر اس مسئلہ کی تمام صورتیں اور اس میں اختلافات کو تفصیل سے بیان کر دیا ہے
If the game is found alive, slaughter is necessary; otherwise it will not be halal. Quduri said: If he (the shooter) finds the game alive, he should slaughter it, and we have explained this with all its aspects and the disagreement in the first chapter, so we will not repeat it. Commentary: If the shooter finds the game alive, then slaughtering is obligatory; otherwise it will not be halal. The author has explained all the aspects of this issue and the differences in the first chapter on page 480 volume 4.

Commentary
This passage states a concise ruling: if a hunter hits game with an arrow and finds it alive, he must perform proper Islamic slaughter (dhabh) on it. If he does not, the meat is not halal. The author references an earlier chapter where all the detailed rulings and differences of opinion were given, indicating that this is a summary.

Key Terms
ذبح (dhabh): Islamic ritual slaughter by cutting the throat and carotid arteries.

Game That Runs Away After Being Hit: Hanafi Ruling
Ruling on game that runs away after arrow hit and found dead later

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on game that is hit, runs away, and is found dead, and apply the distinction between continued pursuit and abandonment.

Core Idea
If you give up pursuit, probability overrides certainty: the game is haram.

Imagine you shoot an arrow and the animal disappears into the woods. You track it for hours and find it dead—halal. But if you give up and return later to find it dead, the Hanafi school says it's not halal. Why? Because the possibility that something else killed it is treated as certain. This lesson unpacks the ruling and the legal reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage and note the two possibilities: (1) hunter continues pursuit and finds game dead, (2) hunter abandons pursuit then finds it dead. The ruling differs.
2
Extract the ruling for each
State the hukm: In scenario 1, the game is halal (eaten). In scenario 2, it is haram (not eaten).
3
Analyze the first proof (hadith)
The Prophet disapproved of eating such game because it might have been killed by vermin. Note that the disapproval is a karahah that implies prohibition in this context.
4
Analyze the second proof (rational)
The text argues that a probable cause (other death) is like certainty in hunting. When the certain arrow hit and the probable other death conflict, prohibition is given precedence.
This is a usul principle: istishab al-haraam when in doubt.
5
Answer the hypothetical objection
How can probability override certainty? The Hanafi response: in hunting, probability is treated as certainty; when in conflict, prohibition prevails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شکار کو تیر لگا لیکن مشقت کر کے بھاگ گیا پھر مردہ ملا تو کیا حکم
قال واذا وقع السهم بالصيد فتحامل حتى غاب عنه ولم يزل في طلبه حتى اصابه ميتا اكل وان قعد عن طلبه ثم اصابه ميتا لم يوكل لماروى عن النبي عليه السلام انه كره اكل الصيد اذا غاب عن الرامي وقال لعل هوام الارض قتلته ولان احتمال الموت بسبب اخر قائم فما ينبغي ان يحل اكله لان الموهوم في هذا كالمتحقق لما روينا
ترجمہ قدوری نے فرمایا اور جب تیر شکار کو لگا ( جس سے وہ گرا) پھر مشقت کے ساتھ وہ بھا گا یہاں تک کہ شکاری سے غائب ہو گیا اور وہ برابر اس کی تلاش میں رہا یہاں تک کہ اس نے اسکو مردہ پایا تو وہ کھایا جائے گا اور اگر وہ اس کی طلب سے بیٹھ رہا پھر اس کو مرا ہوا پایا تو نہیں کھایا جائے گا بوجہ اس کے کہ نبی علیہ السلام سے مروی ہے کہ آپ نے شکار کھانے کو مکروہ جانا جبکہ وہ رامی سے غائب ہو جائے اور فرمایا شاید کہ زمین کے جانوروں نے اس کو مار ڈالا ہو اور اسلئے کہ دوسرے سبب سے موت کا احتمال قائم ہے پس یہ بات مناسب نہیں کہ اس کا کھانا حلال ہو اس لئے کہ موہوم اس باب میں تحقیق کے مثل ہے اس روایت کی وجہ سے جس کو ہم روایت کر چکے ہیں ۔
تشریح شکار کو تیرلگا اور وہ گرا لیکن وہ مشقت برداشت کر کے بھاگا اور غائب ہو گیا پھر وہ مالیکن مرا ہوا ملا تو اب کیا حکم ہے؟ تو دیکھا کہ تیر مارنے والا برابر اس کی تلاش میں ہے یا بیٹھ رہا پہلی صورت میں حلال ہے اور دوسری میں حرام ہے جس کی مصنف نے دو دلیلیں ذکر کی ہیں۔
ا۔ آنحضرت صلى الله عليه وسلم نے اس کے کھانے کو نا پسند فرماتے ہوئے فرمایا شاید کہ حشرات الارض نے اس کو مار دیا ہو۔ -۲- دلیل عقلی یہ ہے کہ احتمال ہے کہ یہ شکار آپ کے تیر کے علاوہ کسی اور سبب سے مر گیا ہو تو پھر اس کا کھانا مناسب نہیں ہے۔
سوال ... آپ نے امر موہوم پر بنیاد کیسے رکھی جبکہ اس کے مقابلہ میں امر تحقق موجود ہے یعنی تیر مارتا جواب ... شکار کے باب میں موہوم متحقیق کے درجہ میں ہے اور جب دونوں مساوی ہو گئے تو تعارض ہوگیا اور بر وقت تعارض حرمت کو ترجیح دی گئی ہے ( کما ہو الاصل)
Question: What is the ruling if the arrow hits the game and it struggles and runs away until it disappears, then found dead? Quduri said: When the arrow hits the game and it struggles until it disappears from him, and he continues to pursue it until he finds it dead, it is eaten. But if he sits down from pursuing it and then finds it dead, it is not eaten, because it is narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he disapproved of eating game when it disappears from the shooter and said, 'Perhaps the insects of the earth killed it.' And because the possibility of death from another cause exists, so it is not appropriate to permit eating it, because the probable in this matter is like the certain, due to the narration we have transmitted. Commentary: The game is hit by an arrow and falls, but it struggles and runs away and disappears. Then the hunter finds it dead. The ruling depends: if the hunter continued to search, it is halal; if he gave up, it is haram. The author gives two proofs: 1) The Prophet disapproved of eating such game, saying perhaps vermin killed it. 2) Rational: there is a possibility that it died from another cause, so eating it is not appropriate. A question arises: How can probable override certain (the arrow hit)? The answer: In the chapter of hunting, the probable is treated as certain. When the two are equal, there is conflict, and at the time of conflict, prohibition is given precedence (as is the principle).
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage addresses a common scenario in hunting: an arrow hits the game, but the animal runs away and is later found dead. The ruling distinguishes two cases: if the hunter continued to pursue it, the game is halal because it is likely the arrow killed it. If the hunter gave up pursuit and later found it dead, it is not halal because of the possibility that other factors caused the death. The proof includes a prophetic tradition and a rational argument. The author also addresses an objection: how can a mere probability override the certainty that the arrow was fired? The response is that in hunting, probability is treated as certainty when it comes to the cause of death, and since both the probability and the certainty are in conflict, the principle of preferring prohibition is applied.

Key Terms
موہوم (mawhum): Probable or conjectural matter; something that is likely but not certain.
متحقق (mutahaqqiq): Certain or established matter; here, the fact that the arrow was fired and hit the animal.
صید (sayd): Game or hunted animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A hunter shoots a bird with a bow. The bird flies away and disappears. The hunter remains sitting and does not search. Two hours later, he sees the dead bird on the ground nearby. According to the Hanafi ruling, can he eat it?
A) Yes, because the arrow hit the bird.
B) Yes, because he did not leave the area.
C) No, because he abandoned pursuit.
D) No, because the bird was not slaughtered.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, for game that is hit by an arrow, runs away, and is found dead, the permissibility depends on whether the hunter continued pursuit; if he gave up, the game is haram.
